beginner

Body is Not a Collection of Accidents: The Core Refutation
Sanusi states the second objective and refutes the view that body is a collection of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the self-evident argument that a body cannot be composed of accidents because accidents do not subsist by themselves.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot stand alone; a collection of them cannot be self-subsistent.

Sanusi begins the second objective with a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that body is merely a collection of accidents. He presents a simple yet powerful argument based on the nature of accidents: they cannot subsist by themselves. This lesson will walk you through the argument and the additional proof from the indivisible substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Sanusi's thesis and the opposing view. Then read the argument that accidents cannot subsist by themselves.
Notice that Sanusi uses the term 'daruri' (self-evident) to emphasize the certainty of the argument.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the direct self-evident proof (accidents need a substance to subsist in) and the indirect proof from the indivisible substance (if composed of accidents, each accident would either be self-subsistent or not).
The second proof is introduced by 'wa qad yustadallu' (and it may be argued).
3
Consider the objection and response
Sanusi anticipates an objection: the whole may have a different rule than its parts. He says this does not apply because the impossibility is clear to every rational person.
This shows the argument is a first principle, not a logical deduction that could be challenged.
4
Reflect on the self-evident nature
Ask yourself why Sanusi considers this argument self-evident. What does it mean for the debate? This solidifies the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم

المقصد الثاني : ليس الجسم مجموع أعراض مجتمعة خلافاً للنظام والنجار من المعتزلة فإنهما ذهبا إلى أن الجواهر مطلقاً أعراض مجتمعة وهذا باطل لما علمت أن العرض لا يقوم بذاته سواء كان واحداً أو متعدداً بالغاً ما بلغ فلا بد من انتهائه إلى جوهر يقوم به فلا يكون الجوهر القائم بذاته مجموع أعراض وحدها وبالجملة فبطلانه ضروري إذ كل عاقل يعلم أن الأمر المجتمع من أمور يمتنع قيامها بنفسها لا يكون قائماً بذاته بل محتاجاً إلى أمر آخر يقوم به وما ذكرناه تنبيه على الحكم البديهي فلا يتجه عليه أن الكل من حيث هو كل قد يخالف حكمه حكم كل واحد منه وقد يستدل على امتناع تركب الجوهر من العرض بأن الجوهر الفرد متحيز بالاتفاق فلو كان مركباً من الأعراض فكل واحد من تلك الأعراض إما أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات فهو جوهر ويلزم

قوله : (خلافاً للنظام) هذا موافق لما هو المذكور في كتب المعتزلة من أن الجسم عند النظام مركب من اللون والطعم والرائحة ونحو ذلك من الأعراض، فقيل في الجمع بين هذا القول منه، والقول منه بتركبه من الأجزاء الغير المتناهية أن الجوهر الفرد عنده مركب من الأعراض أو أن له قولين، لكن المذكور في شرح المقاصد أن الظاهر من كتبهم أن مثل الأكوان والاعتقادات والآلام والذات وما أشبه ذلك أعراض لا دخل لها في حقيقة الجسم وفاقاً، وأما الألوان والأضواء والطعوم والروائح والأصوات والكيفيات الملموسة من الحرارة والبرودة وغيرهما فعند النظام جواهر بل أجسام حتى صرح بأن كل واحد من ذلك لطيف، وإذا اجتمعت وتداخلت حصل الجسم الكثيف، وعند الجمهور كذلك أعراض لأن الجسم عند ضرار بن عمرو والحسين النجار مجموع تلك الأعراض وعند الأخيرين جواهر مجتمعة تحلها تلك الأعراض، فما وقع في المواقف خلافاً للنظام ليس على ما ينبغي والصواب مكان النظام ضرار، فعلى هذا لا الجمعية عليه بأن الأمر المجموع من أمور غير قائمة بذاتها يمتنع أن يكون قائماً بالذات كما لا يخفى . من جسم يتم قوله : (إما أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات فهو جوهر إذ لا معنى للجوهر عند المتكلمين إلا المتحيز بالذات، فلا يرد أنه الاتفاق على كل جوهر متحيز لا يستلزم القول بأن كل متحيز جوهر مع أن صحة الاستدلال موقوف عليه .
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Second Objective: Body is not a collection of accidents, contrary to an-Nazzam and an-Najjar among the Mu'tazila. For both of them held that substances (jawahir) absolutely are accidents collected together. But this is false, because you have known that an accident (arad) does not subsist by itself, whether it is one or multiple, whatever degree it reaches. So it must terminate at a substance (jawhar) that subsists by it, so the substance that subsists by itself cannot be a collection of accidents alone. In short, its falsity is necessary (daruri), for every rational person knows that a thing compounded of things whose self-subsistence is impossible is not self-subsistent, but rather needs another thing to subsist by. And what we mentioned is a reminder of the self-evident judgment, so the objection that the whole, qua whole, may have a different judgment from each one of its parts does not apply to it. And it may be argued for the impossibility of the composition of substance from accident by [the fact] that the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) occupies space (mutahayyiz) by agreement. So if it were composed of accidents, then each one of those accidents is either self-subsistent in occupying space — then it is a substance — and it follows... His saying: ('Contrary to an-Nazzam') This agrees with what is mentioned in the books of the Mu'tazila, that body according to an-Nazzam is composed of color, taste, smell, and similar accidents. So it was said in reconciling this statement of his with his statement that it is composed of infinite parts: that the indivisible substance according to him is composed of accidents, or that he has two opinions. However, what is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is that the apparent from their books is that things like modes (akwan), beliefs, pains, essence (dhat), and the like are accidents that have no involvement in the reality of the body, by agreement. As for colors, lights, tastes, smells, sounds, and tangible qualities like heat, cold, and others, according to an-Nazzam they are substances, indeed bodies, so much so that he stated that each one of them is subtle, and when they gather and interpenetrate, a dense body results. According to the majority, they are also accidents because body according to Dirar ibn 'Amr and al-Husayn al-Najjar is a collection of those accidents, and according to the latter two [al-Najjar and an-Nazzam?] they are substances gathered together that are solved by those accidents. So what occurs in al-Mawaqif saying 'contrary to an-Nazzam' is not as it should be; the correct [name] in place of an-Nazzam is Dirar. Based on this, there is no [objection] against it that a thing compounded of things that are not self-subsistent is impossible to be self-subsistent, as is not hidden. From the body is completed? His saying: ('Either it is self-subsistent in occupying space, then it is a substance') because there is no meaning of substance according to the theologians except that which occupies space by itself. So it is not objected that the agreement that every substance occupies space does not necessitate the statement that every occupant of space is a substance, even though the validity of the inference depends on it.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is both a refutation and a historical clarification. It begins with the statement of the second objective: body is not a collection of accidents. The commentator identifies the opponents: an-Nazzam and an-Najjar of the Mu'tazila, who hold that all substances are merely collections of accidents. The refutation is presented in two parts. First, a direct argument from the nature of accident: every accident cannot subsist by itself, so a collection of such non-self-subsistent accidents cannot constitute a self-subsistent substance. The commentator underscores that this is a self-evident (daruri) truth, known to every rational person, and thus immune to the objection that a whole may have a rule different from its parts. Second, an additional proof from the notion of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard): if it were composed of accidents, each accident would either be self-subsistent in occupying space (thus itself a substance) or not, leading to a regress. The argument is cut off at 'and it follows' (wa yalzam), but the point is clear. The passage then offers a marginal explanation of the historical context: the view attributed to an-Nazzam is that body is composed of accidents like color, taste, etc. The commentator clarifies that according to Sharh al-Maqasid, an-Nazzam actually considered colors and the like as subtle bodies, not accidents. Thus the correct attribution of the view that body is a collection of accidents is to Dirar ibn 'Amr, not an-Nazzam. This emendation is important for accurate historical understanding. The note concludes by reaffirming the original argument: a collection of non-self-subsistent things cannot be self-subsistent.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)
متحيز (occupying space)
جوهر فرد (indivisible substance)
ضروري (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think the argument is circular or that the whole can have a different rule than its parts.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it relies on a self-evident premise that accidents cannot subsist by themselves. Sanusi explicitly addresses the objection about the whole and parts, stating that it does not apply because the impossibility is clear.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that body cannot be a collection of accidents?
A) Because accidents are not real.
B) Because a collection of non-self-subsistent things cannot be self-subsistent.
C) Because the indivisible substance is not composed of accidents.
D) Because an-Nazzam and an-Najjar were not reliable authorities.

Key Takeaway
The body's self-subsistence is incompatible with being a mere collection of accidents; this is a necessary truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If accidents cannot subsist without a substance, and you are a substance upheld by Allah every moment, how does this truth expose any false sense of independence you have? Will you today consciously acknowledge your absolute need for Him in every breath and thought?
?
The passage calls the falsity of the Mu'tazila view 'necessary' and known to every rational person. Is there an area of your life where you are suppressing a clear rational truth because it demands a change? What would it cost you to fully accept and act on that truth today?
?
You are not just a collection of random experiences and accidents; you are a coherent being sustained by the Creator. How does this reshape your understanding of your identity, purpose, and accountability? Can you think of one concrete way to live out this truth in your next action?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Historical Clarification and Defense of the Proof
Commentator corrects historical attribution and defends the proof from indivisible substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical correction regarding an-Nazzam's view and the clarification of the proof from indivisible substance.

Core Idea
Accurate attribution matters: the view that body is a collection of accidents belongs to Dirar, not an-Nazzam.

The commentator provides an important historical note: the view that body is a collection of accidents is more accurately attributed to Dirar ibn 'Amr, not an-Nazzam. He also clarifies a potential objection to the proof from indivisible substance, ensuring the argument's validity by defining substance as that which occupies space by itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical correction carefully
Notice how the commentator reconciles different reports about an-Nazzam's view. He references Sharh al-Maqasid to show that an-Nazzam considered colors and the like as subtle bodies, not accidents.
This explains why the attribution in al-Mawaqif is considered incorrect.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator distinguishes between accidents (like modes, beliefs) and subtle bodies (like colors, tastes) according to an-Nazzam. This clarifies the actual position.
3
Examine the defense of the proof
The commentator addresses an objection: the inference from 'every substance occupies space' to 'every occupant of space is a substance' is not universally valid. He explains that in this context, substance is defined precisely as that which occupies space by itself, so the converse holds.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Accurate historical attribution prevents misrepresentation of opponents and strengthens the integrity of the argument. The proof from indivisible substance remains sound when terms are properly defined.

Source Text

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم

المقصد الثاني : ليس الجسم مجموع أعراض مجتمعة خلافاً للنظام والنجار من المعتزلة فإنهما ذهبا إلى أن الجواهر مطلقاً أعراض مجتمعة وهذا باطل لما علمت أن العرض لا يقوم بذاته سواء كان واحداً أو متعدداً بالغاً ما بلغ فلا بد من انتهائه إلى جوهر يقوم به فلا يكون الجوهر القائم بذاته مجموع أعراض وحدها وبالجملة فبطلانه ضروري إذ كل عاقل يعلم أن الأمر المجتمع من أمور يمتنع قيامها بنفسها لا يكون قائماً بذاته بل محتاجاً إلى أمر آخر يقوم به وما ذكرناه تنبيه على الحكم البديهي فلا يتجه عليه أن الكل من حيث هو كل قد يخالف حكمه حكم كل واحد منه وقد يستدل على امتناع تركب الجوهر من العرض بأن الجوهر الفرد متحيز بالاتفاق فلو كان مركباً من الأعراض فكل واحد من تلك الأعراض إما أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات فهو جوهر ويلزم

قوله : (خلافاً للنظام) هذا موافق لما هو المذكور في كتب المعتزلة من أن الجسم عند النظام مركب من اللون والطعم والرائحة ونحو ذلك من الأعراض، فقيل في الجمع بين هذا القول منه، والقول منه بتركبه من الأجزاء الغير المتناهية أن الجوهر الفرد عنده مركب من الأعراض أو أن له قولين، لكن المذكور في شرح المقاصد أن الظاهر من كتبهم أن مثل الأكوان والاعتقادات والآلام والذات وما أشبه ذلك أعراض لا دخل لها في حقيقة الجسم وفاقاً، وأما الألوان والأضواء والطعوم والروائح والأصوات والكيفيات الملموسة من الحرارة والبرودة وغيرهما فعند النظام جواهر بل أجسام حتى صرح بأن كل واحد من ذلك لطيف، وإذا اجتمعت وتداخلت حصل الجسم الكثيف، وعند الجمهور كذلك أعراض لأن الجسم عند ضرار بن عمرو والحسين النجار مجموع تلك الأعراض وعند الأخيرين جواهر مجتمعة تحلها تلك الأعراض، فما وقع في المواقف خلافاً للنظام ليس على ما ينبغي والصواب مكان النظام ضرار، فعلى هذا لا الجمعية عليه بأن الأمر المجموع من أمور غير قائمة بذاتها يمتنع أن يكون قائماً بالذات كما لا يخفى . من جسم يتم قوله : (إما أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات فهو جوهر إذ لا معنى للجوهر عند المتكلمين إلا المتحيز بالذات، فلا يرد أنه الاتفاق على كل جوهر متحيز لا يستلزم القول بأن كل متحيز جوهر مع أن صحة الاستدلال موقوف عليه .
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Second Objective: Body is not a collection of accidents, contrary to an-Nazzam and an-Najjar among the Mu'tazila. For both of them held that substances (jawahir) absolutely are accidents collected together. But this is false, because you have known that an accident (arad) does not subsist by itself, whether it is one or multiple, whatever degree it reaches. So it must terminate at a substance (jawhar) that subsists by it, so the substance that subsists by itself cannot be a collection of accidents alone. In short, its falsity is necessary (daruri), for every rational person knows that a thing compounded of things whose self-subsistence is impossible is not self-subsistent, but rather needs another thing to subsist by. And what we mentioned is a reminder of the self-evident judgment, so the objection that the whole, qua whole, may have a different judgment from each one of its parts does not apply to it. And it may be argued for the impossibility of the composition of substance from accident by [the fact] that the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) occupies space (mutahayyiz) by agreement. So if it were composed of accidents, then each one of those accidents is either self-subsistent in occupying space — then it is a substance — and it follows... His saying: ('Contrary to an-Nazzam') This agrees with what is mentioned in the books of the Mu'tazila, that body according to an-Nazzam is composed of color, taste, smell, and similar accidents. So it was said in reconciling this statement of his with his statement that it is composed of infinite parts: that the indivisible substance according to him is composed of accidents, or that he has two opinions. However, what is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is that the apparent from their books is that things like modes (akwan), beliefs, pains, essence (dhat), and the like are accidents that have no involvement in the reality of the body, by agreement. As for colors, lights, tastes, smells, sounds, and tangible qualities like heat, cold, and others, according to an-Nazzam they are substances, indeed bodies, so much so that he stated that each one of them is subtle, and when they gather and interpenetrate, a dense body results. According to the majority, they are also accidents because body according to Dirar ibn 'Amr and al-Husayn al-Najjar is a collection of those accidents, and according to the latter two [al-Najjar and an-Nazzam?] they are substances gathered together that are solved by those accidents. So what occurs in al-Mawaqif saying 'contrary to an-Nazzam' is not as it should be; the correct [name] in place of an-Nazzam is Dirar. Based on this, there is no [objection] against it that a thing compounded of things that are not self-subsistent is impossible to be self-subsistent, as is not hidden. From the body is completed? His saying: ('Either it is self-subsistent in occupying space, then it is a substance') because there is no meaning of substance according to the theologians except that which occupies space by itself. So it is not objected that the agreement that every substance occupies space does not necessitate the statement that every occupant of space is a substance, even though the validity of the inference depends on it.

Commentary
This passage is both a refutation and a historical clarification. It begins with the statement of the second objective: body is not a collection of accidents. The commentator identifies the opponents: an-Nazzam and an-Najjar of the Mu'tazila, who hold that all substances are merely collections of accidents. The refutation is presented in two parts. First, a direct argument from the nature of accident: every accident cannot subsist by itself, so a collection of such non-self-subsistent accidents cannot constitute a self-subsistent substance. The commentator underscores that this is a self-evident (daruri) truth, known to every rational person, and thus immune to the objection that a whole may have a rule different from its parts. Second, an additional proof from the notion of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard): if it were composed of accidents, each accident would either be self-subsistent in occupying space (thus itself a substance) or not, leading to a regress. The argument is cut off at 'and it follows' (wa yalzam), but the point is clear. The passage then offers a marginal explanation of the historical context: the view attributed to an-Nazzam is that body is composed of accidents like color, taste, etc. The commentator clarifies that according to Sharh al-Maqasid, an-Nazzam actually considered colors and the like as subtle bodies, not accidents. Thus the correct attribution of the view that body is a collection of accidents is to Dirar ibn 'Amr, not an-Nazzam. This emendation is important for accurate historical understanding. The note concludes by reaffirming the original argument: a collection of non-self-subsistent things cannot be self-subsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a view to a scholar without verifying it? How does this lesson's emphasis on correct attribution challenge you to be more careful in your own statements about scholars and their positions? Will you commit to verifying attributions before repeating them?
?
The proof from indivisible substance relies on a clear definition of 'substance.' In your own life, are there areas where you hold beliefs based on unclear or unexamined definitions? What is one concept you need to define more carefully, and what step will you take to do so?
?
The commentator corrects an error in a respected work like al-Mawaqif. When you encounter a mistake in a source you trust, do you meekly accept it or do you seek correction? What is one claim you've heard about a theological topic that you should verify today?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
4 min
The Definition of Jawhar and Its Scope
Commentator defines jawhar and clarifies the scope of the term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun define jawhar as 'that which is extended by itself' and why this definition prevents a potential objection to the inference.

Core Idea
A substance in kalam is simply whatever occupies space by itself.

Before the argument about atoms can proceed, the commentator establishes a precise definition of 'jawhar' (substance). For the classical Islamic theologians, this term has a technical meaning that differs from the philosophical tradition. By defining it as 'extended by itself', the commentator forestalls an objection that would otherwise undermine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first note on the definition of substance
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the commentator directly gives the definition of jawhar as 'that which is extended by itself' (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhat).
This is the key definition used by the mutakallimun.
2
Understand the objection that is prevented
The commentator states that the definition avoids the objection that while every substance is extended, not every extended thing is a substance. The inference requires the stronger claim that every extended thing is a substance, but the theologians define substance as exactly that.
The inference works because the definition makes 'extended' equivalent to 'substance'.
3
Read the second note clarifying 'absolutely'
The commentator clarifies that 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) in the argument includes both bodies and indivisible parts. This ensures the term covers all possible forms of extended things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إما أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات فهو (جوهر إذ لا معنى للجوهر عند المتكلمين إلا المتحيز بالذات، فلا يرد أنه الاتفاق على كل جوهر متحيز لا يستلزم القول بأن كل متحيز جوهر مع أن صحة الاستدلال موقوف عليه .

قوله : (مطلقاً) جسماً كان أو جزءاً لا يتجزأ .

قوله : (إن الجواهر من حيث هي جواهر أي مع قطع النظر عن عوارضها .

قوله : ( وإلا كانت إلخ) إشارة إلى أن الدليل قياس استثنائي وليس قياساً اقترانياً على هيئة الشكل الثاني كما يتبادر من ظاهر العبارة، لأن النتيجة حينئذ لا يتحصل شيء من الأجسام من الجواهر الفردة لأنه ليس مركباً، وتقريره أنه لو كان الأجسام عبارة عن الجواهر المؤتلفة لكانت متماثلة والتالي باطل أما الملازمة فلان الجواهر متماثلة وأما بطلان التالي فلان الأجسام مختلفة فالمقدمة الأولى لإثبات الملازمة والثانية لإبطال التالي . قوله : ( فتكون أعراضاً ) أي إذا لم تكن الأجسام جواهر مؤتلفة تكون أعراضاً مجتمعة إذ الممكن الموجود منحصر في الجوهر والعرض، ويرد عليه أنه يجوز أن يكون مركباً من الجواهر والأعراض.

قوله : (لا نسلم أن الجوهر إلخ في شرح المقاصد هذا الجواب لا يتم على مذهب المانعين، ويتم إلزاماً لأن النظام قائل بتماثل الجواهر الفردة. الأقرب منع اختلاف الأجسام

قوله : (فلا يكون جوهراً فرداً فيه بحث لأن معنى الجوهر الفرد ما لا ينقسم بحسب المقدار أصلاً وهو لا ينافي أن يكون له أجزاء كالهيولى والصورة للجسم.

قوله : ( ومن المعلوم أن ضم ما لا يتحيز إلخ) فيه بحث لأن قوله : أو لا يكون متحيزاً رفع الإيجاب الكلي، فيجوز أن يكون بعضها متحيزاً بالذات فلا يلزم ما ذكره من المحذور ويمكن أن يدفع بأن المقصود إبطال مذهب النظام القائل بتركب الجوهر من محض الأعراض، وإنما لم يقتصر على الشق الثاني مع أنه كاف في المقصود توسيعاً للدائرة فلا يضر عدم تصريحه بإبطال ما ذكر من الاحتمال المحض .

قوله : (الأول) أن الجوهر (إلخ هذا الوجه على تقدير تمامه لا يثبت مذهبها أعني كون الجسم محض الأعراض المجتمعة بل إنما يثبت كون الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم وفي قول الشارح فلا حاجة بنا حينئذ إلى دخول الأعراض في حقائق الجواهر إشارة إلى هذا.
His saying: (Either it is essentially extended) so it is a substance, for there is no meaning of substance (jawhar) according to the theologians (mutakallimun) except that which is extended by itself. So it is not objected that the agreement that every substance is extended does not entail that every extended thing is a substance, while the validity of the inference depends on it.

His saying: (Absolutely) whether it is a body or an indivisible part.

His saying: (Atoms as such) meaning with abstraction from their accidents.

His saying: (Otherwise they would be etc.) is an indication that the proof is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and not a conjunctive syllogism in the form of the second figure as might appear from the apparent wording, because the conclusion then would be that no body is composed of indivisible atoms since it is not composite. Its exposition: if bodies were composed of atoms, they would be similar, and the consequent is false. The implication is because atoms are similar, and the falsehood of the consequent is because bodies are different. So the first premise establishes the implication and the second refutes the consequent. His saying: (Thus they would be accidents) means if bodies are not composed of atoms, they would be gathered accidents, since existing possible things are confined to substance and accident. But it is objected that it is possible for them to be composed of substances and accidents.

His saying: (We do not accept that the substance etc.) in Sharh al-Maqasid: this answer is not sound according to the school of those who deny, but it works as a necessary consequence because an-Nazzam affirms the similarity of indivisible atoms. The closest is to deny the difference of bodies.

His saying: (So it would not be an indivisible atom) is debatable because the meaning of indivisible atom is that which is not divisible in terms of magnitude at all, and that does not preclude it having parts like matter and form for the body.

His saying: (And it is known that combining what is not extended etc.) is debatable because his saying 'or it is not essentially extended' lifts the universal affirmative, so it is possible that some of them are essentially extended, so the mentioned problem does not arise. It can be defended by saying that the intent is to refute the school of an-Nazzam who holds that the atom is composed of pure accidents; he did not restrict himself to the second disjunct even though it was sufficient for the purpose, expanding the scope, so the lack of explicit refutation of the mentioned mere possibility does not harm.

His saying: (First) that the substance etc.: This point, even if complete, does not establish their school, namely that the body is pure gathered accidents, but rather establishes that accidents enter into the reality of the body. In the words of the commentator, we have no need then for the entry of accidents into the realities of atoms, indicating this.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a series of commentator's notes on specific phrases from the main argument. Each note clarifies a point of definition, logical structure, or response to objections.

1. The first note defines substance (jawhar) for theologians as that which is extended by itself, addressing a possible objection about the inference.
2. The second note clarifies that 'absolutely' refers to both body and indivisible part.
3. The third note explains that 'atoms as such' means abstracting from their accidents.
4. The fourth note discusses the logical form: the argument is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), not a conjunctive syllogism. It explains how the premises work: the first establishes the implication, the second refutes the consequent. It also notes an objection that the body could be composed of both substances and accidents.
5. The fifth note comments on the response 'we do not accept that atoms are homogeneous', saying it is only effective as a necessary consequence against an-Nazzam who holds atoms are similar.
6. The sixth note debates the definition of indivisible atom, arguing that being indivisible in magnitude does not preclude having parts like matter and form.
7. The seventh note critiques the argument about combining non-extended things, pointing out that the second disjunct ('or not essentially extended') does not necessarily lead to the problem, but the author's intent was to refute an-Nazzam's specific view.
8. The eighth note argues that the opponent's first point does not prove that the body is pure accidents, only that accidents are part of its reality, which does not require accidents to enter into the essences of atoms.

Key Terms
جوهر
تحيز
مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'substance' in kalam is the same as in Aristotelian philosophy, where substance is the subject of accidents and not defined by extension.
Correction: The mutakallimun define substance functionally as whatever is extended by itself, which solves logical problems in their arguments about atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the definition of substance (jawhar) in the terminology of the mutakallimun?
A) That which subsists by itself and is the subject of accidents
B) That which is extended by itself (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhat)
C) A body that is indivisible
D) An atom that occupies space

Key Takeaway
For the mutakallimun, a substance is defined as anything that is essentially extended, making extension and substance synonymous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the word 'substance' or 'essence' without knowing its precise theological meaning? How might using loose definitions affect your understanding of important creedal issues? Take a moment to write down one concept in your faith that you have not defined rigorously and commit to learning its technical definition.
?
The commentator carefully addresses a potential objection to his definition. When you learn a new Islamic concept, do you actively question it and consider counterarguments, or do you just accept it? What is one belief you hold that you have never examined from a critical perspective? Make a plan to study it with a teacher or text that presents alternative views.
?
If every physical thing, including your own body, is defined merely as 'extended substance', how does that change the way you view your relationship with your body and the material world? Are you attached to your body as an end in itself, or do you see it as a sign of the Creator? How can you cultivate a mindset of seeing the physical world as a means, not an ultimate goal?

The Logical Form: Conditional Syllogism
Commentator clarifies the logical form of the argument and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the argument about atoms and bodies is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and how its premises work together.

Core Idea
The argument is a conditional syllogism: if bodies were atoms, they'd be similar—but they're not.

The commentator now examines the logical structure of the argument that bodies are composed of atoms. He clarifies that it is not a simple categorical syllogism but a conditional one. He also addresses an objection about the composition of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the note on the logical form
Read the commentator's explanation of the argument's structure. Notice that he says it is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), not a conjunctive syllogism in the second figure.
The conditional syllogism is also known as 'modus tollens' or 'modus ponens'.
2
Understand the exposition of the premises
The commentator explains how the premises work: the first premise establishes the implication (if bodies are composed of atoms, then bodies are similar), and the second premise refutes the consequent (but bodies are different, so the antecedent is false).
This is a classic modus tollens.
3
Consider the objection about composition
After presenting the logical form, the commentator notes an objection: it is possible that bodies are composed of both substances and accidents, not just atoms. This objection challenges the conclusion.

Source Text

قوله : (مطلقاً) جسماً كان أو جزءاً لا يتجزأ .

قوله : (إن الجواهر من حيث هي جواهر أي مع قطع النظر عن عوارضها .

Refining the Notion of Indivisible Atom
Commentator discusses the definition of indivisible atom and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether an indivisible atom can have parts like matter and form, and how the commentator responds to an argument about non-extended things.

Core Idea
An indivisible atom is indivisible in magnitude, but may still have metaphysical parts.

The commentator continues with more subtle points. He examines the definition of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) and argues that being indivisible in magnitude does not preclude having parts like matter and form. He also critiques an argument about combining non-extended things and clarifies that the opponent's first point does not prove their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the note on the indivisible atom
Read the passage defining the indivisible atom. Notice that the commentator argues that the definition (what cannot be divided in magnitude) does not rule out having parts like hyle (matter) and form.
This is a key distinction between physical indivisibility and metaphysical composition.
2
Understand the critique of the argument about non-extended things
The commentator critiques an argument that combines non-extended things. He points out a logical flaw: the argument uses a universal negative, which does not preclude some things being extended.
The commentator also offers a defense that the argument was specifically aimed at an-Nazzam's view.
3
Read the final note on the opponent's first point
The commentator notes that even if the opponent's first point were accepted, it only shows that accidents enter into the reality of the body, not that the body is pure accidents. This does not establish the opponent's position.

His saying: (Either it is essentially extended) so it is a substance, for there is no meaning of substance (jawhar) according to the theologians (mutakallimun) except that which is extended by itself. So it is not objected that the agreement that every substance is extended does not entail that every extended thing is a substance, while the validity of the inference depends on it.

Commentary
This passage is a series of commentator's notes on specific phrases from the main argument. Each note clarifies a point of definition, logical structure, or response to objections.

1. The first note defines substance (jawhar) for theologians as that which is extended by itself, addressing a possible objection about the inference.
2. The second note clarifies that 'absolutely' refers to both body and indivisible part.
3. The third note explains that 'atoms as such' means abstracting from their accidents.
4. The fourth note discusses the logical form: the argument is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), not a conjunctive syllogism. It explains how the premises work: the first establishes the implication, the second refutes the consequent. It also notes an objection that the body could be composed of both substances and accidents.
5. The fifth note comments on the response 'we do not accept that atoms are homogeneous', saying it is only effective as a necessary consequence against an-Nazzam who holds atoms are similar.
6. The sixth note debates the definition of indivisible atom, arguing that being indivisible in magnitude does not preclude having parts like matter and form.
7. The seventh note critiques the argument about combining non-extended things, pointing out that the second disjunct ('or not essentially extended') does not necessarily lead to the problem, but the author's intent was to refute an-Nazzam's specific view.
8. The eighth note argues that the opponent's first point does not prove that the body is pure accidents, only that accidents are part of its reality, which does not require accidents to enter into the essences of atoms.

الهيولى
صورة
تحيز
أعراض

Correction: The commentator clarifies that it means indivisible in magnitude, but it can still have metaphysical parts like matter and form.

Proof from Homogeneity that Body is Not a Collection of Accidents
Al-Amidi refutes the claim that body is a collection of accidents using the homogeneity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi uses the homogeneity argument to refute the claim that a body is a mere collection of accidents.

Core Idea
Homogeneous atoms cannot produce diverse bodies—so body is not just a collection of atoms.

In this passage, al-Amidi presents a proof from homogeneity to argue that a body cannot be a mere collection of accidents. The argument relies on the essential attributes of atoms and the observable diversity of bodies. We will follow the structure of the proof and the response from the Mutakallimun.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Note the technical terms like al-jawhar al-fard and al-tahayyuz.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The passage begins by stating what the refutation aims to disprove: that the body is a collection of accidents (a view attributed to al-Nazzam).
The claim is presented indirectly through the consequence that the indivisible atom would be composed.
3
Understand the premise of the proof
The first proof assumes that atoms qua atoms are homogeneous because they share essential attributes. List these attributes: occupying space, self-subsistence, receiving accidents.
Homogeneity means they are identical in essence.
4
See the conclusion drawn
Since bodies like fire, air, and water are diverse, if they were composed of homogeneous atoms they would all be similar. Therefore, bodies cannot be expressions of composed atoms.
The conclusion directly refutes the claim that body is a collection of atoms.
5
Examine the response
The response (qulna) rejects the premise that atoms are homogeneous. According to the Mutakallimun, atoms differ in their essences, thus the argument fails.
This is a typical dialectical move: attack the premise.

Source Text

The First Observatory - The Second Aim: the body is not a collection of accidents. From it: that the indivisible atom would be composed of atoms, so it would not be an indivisible atom and would not be inherently occupying space. It is known that combining what does not occupy space with what does not occupy space does not necessitate occupying space. Al-Amidi refuted it by the possibility that the combination is a condition for occupying space. They argued with two proofs: The first: that atoms qua atoms are homogeneous due to their sharing in the attributes of the atom itself, which are occupying space, self-subsistence, and receiving accidents. And bodies like fire, air, and water are necessarily different. So bodies are not expressions of composed atoms; otherwise they would be similar, and then they would be a collection of accidents. We said: we do not concede that atoms are homogeneous; rather, according to us atoms are different in their essences, and what was mentioned...
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the body is not a collection of accidents (a view attributed to al-Nazzam). The argument begins by stating the problem: if the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) were composed of atoms, it would not be indivisible and would not be inherently occupying space. Al-Amidi refutes this by allowing that combination could be a condition for occupying space. Then the first proof is given: atoms are homogeneous because they share essential attributes (occupying space, self-subsistence, receiving accidents). But bodies (like fire, air, water) are necessarily different. Therefore, bodies cannot be composed of atoms alone; otherwise they would be alike and would be mere collections of accidents. The response (qulna) rejects the premise: atoms are not homogeneous; they differ in their essences. The explanation follows the order of the argument: first the claim, then the proof, then the refutation.

Necessity of Accidents in Body Definition for Homogeneous Atoms
Al-Jurjani argues for the necessary inclusion of accidents in body definition for homogeneity proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Jurjani, anyone who affirms the homogeneity of atoms must include accidents in the definition of body to account for bodily diversity.

Core Idea
If atoms are all alike, bodies differ only by accidents.

This passage tackles a crucial implication of the atomic theory in kalām. Al-Jurjani argues that even if one accepts that all atoms are essentially identical, the evident diversity of bodies forces us to admit that accidents are part of the body's reality. The discussion highlights a key difference between those who allow distinct atom essences and those who do not.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: an initial claim, an objection, and a conclusion for homogeneity proponents.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues in the commentary.
2
Identify the objection presented
Find where an objection is raised: if accidents do not subsist, how can they be part of subsistent atoms? Understand the logical force of this objection.
This objection targets the inclusion of accidents in the definition of body.
3
Understand the inescapable conclusion for Ash'aris and Mu'tazila
The author states that for those who affirm atom homogeneity (like Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila), there is no escape from including accidents in the reality of body. Think about why this is necessary to explain diversity.
Without accidents, all bodies would be identical.

The sharing of atoms in the mentioned attributes does not indicate their similarity in reality because it is possible that those attributes are common accidents shared among their different realities. So we have no need then to include accidents in the realities of atoms. (And therefore) i.e., because of their non-inclusion in them according to us, (we said:) that accidents do not subsist as has passed, and atoms subsist as will come. And it is not hidden that it can be made as an objection by saying: accidents are not subsistent, so they are not included in the subsistent atoms because the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole. Commentary
This passage addresses the consequence of affirming the homogeneity of atoms. It begins by stating that sharing attributes (like occupying space, self-subsistence, receiving accidents) does not imply real similarity, as these could be common accidents across different essences. Thus, one need not include accidents in the essence of atoms. However, the author then presents an objection: if accidents do not persist but atoms do, then accidents cannot be parts of atoms. But for those who affirm homogeneity (Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila), there is no escape from including accidents in the definition of body, because homogeneous atoms alone would produce all bodies alike. Therefore, body must be a combination of an atom and a set of accidents. The argument proceeds by showing that if atoms were the same, bodies would be identical, so diversity must come from accidents. The commentary explains this as a necessary implication of the homogeneity view.

The Body Is Not a Collection of Accidents: Core Argument and Objection
Al-Jurjani presents the argument and the initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument that the body is not a collection of accidents, and the objection based on the non-endurance of accidents.

Core Idea
The body endures; accidents do not. Therefore, the body cannot be a collection of accidents.

This lesson presents the foundational claim that the body is not simply an aggregate of accidents. The author begins by arguing that shared attributes among substances do not prove they are identical, because those attributes could be common accidents. He then states his own position: accidents do not endure, while substances do. However, an objection arises: if accidents are non-enduring and are parts of the body, then the body should also be non-enduring. This lesson sets up that tension, which the subsequent lesson will resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's confident tone in stating the proposition.
2
Identify the main claim and supporting argument
The author states the thesis that the body is not a collection of accidents. He argues that shared attributes do not imply identical realities because they may be common accidents.
3
Observe the author's position on endurance
The author asserts that accidents do not endure (as previously established) while substances do endure. This is a key distinction.
Remember this is a foundational claim in kalam.
4
Understand the objection
The author raises a natural objection: if accidents are non-enduring and are parts of the body, then the whole (the body) should be non-enduring. This objection challenges the coherence of the thesis.
The objection is based on the principle 'negation of part necessitates negation of whole'.

First Observatory - Second Purpose: The body is not a collection of assembled accidents. The sharing of substances in the mentioned attributes does not indicate their similarity in reality, because it is possible that those attributes are general accidents shared among their diverse realities. Hence we have no need then to include accidents in the realities of substances. (Therefore) i.e., because of their non-inclusion in them according to us, (we say:) Accidents do not endure, as previously mentioned, and substances endure, as will come. And it is not hidden that it can be made an objection by saying: Accidents are non-enduring, so they cannot be included in the enduring substances, because the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole. Know that there is no escape for one who acknowledges the homogeneity of individual substances and their similarity in reality—such as the Ash'aris collectively and most Mu'tazila—from including accidents in the reality of the body, so then the body is a substance with a set of accidents attached to that substance, because if it were composed solely of homogeneous substances, all bodies would be identical in essence, but rather they differ according to the accidents dependent on the will of the Chosen One. That difference is the doctrine of al-Nazzam, but some Mu'tazila do not affirm the similarity of substances, and the answer is completed according to their doctrine. And the claim that all bodies are similar by having the attributes of subsistence, occupying space, existing per se, accepting accidents, etc.—what bodies share and what is beyond that of caused attributes—is obstinacy. His saying: (to the inclusion of accidents and their composition from them). Complete on his saying: (If you make an objection) i.e., your proof, although it indicates that bodies are assembled accidents according to us, something negates it: if bodies were assembled accidents, they would be non-enduring because accidents are non-enduring and they are parts of bodies, and the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole. Then this objection does not apply to al-Nazzam according to what the commentator of al-Maqasid summarized by saying: bodies are also renewed, so the body according to them is etc. And the summary of the necessary non-endurance of bodies—since the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole—applies only if one says its inclusion is a specific set, not a particular set, but any set of similar renewed accidents, as the philosophers said in the endurance of matter through the corporeal form; otherwise no. His saying: (objection by saying etc.) In it, this objection is not valid according to al-Nazzam's doctrine because bodies are non-enduring according to him like accidents. And it can be said: the discussion is investigative, not obligating, and the endurance of bodies is necessary, so the opponent's non-acceptance does not harm; but there is something in it. His saying: (about including accidents in the reality of the body and about the lack of distinction between substances and accidents in renewal and endurance, since the renewal of the part necessitates the renewal of the whole, so one must turn to the fact that the one who denies the endurance of accidents is al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, and he does not affirm the similarity of substances, but rather existents according to him are diverse realities. As for the Ash'aris, they affirm their endurance. And you are aware that this contradicts what preceded in the investigations of accidents that al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers among the verifying Ash'aris affirm non-endurance. The truth is that the one who affirms the similarity of individual substances should choose the similarity of bodies, and that the distinction between them is by matters external to their reality.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument combined with objection-and-answer. The author first states the proposition: the body is not a collection of assembled accidents. He then argues that the fact that substances share certain attributes does not prove they are identical in reality, because those attributes could be general accidents common to diverse realities. Therefore, we do not need to include accidents in the essence of substances. The author then presents his own view: accidents do not endure (as previously established), while substances do endure. He anticipates an objection: if accidents are non-enduring, they cannot be part of enduring substances, because the removal of a part removes the whole. The author responds by noting that anyone who affirms the homogeneity of substances (like the Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila) must include accidents in the definition of body, otherwise bodies would all be identical. He then comments on al-Nazzam's position and the view of some Mu'tazila who deny the similarity of substances. The commentary further explains the objection and its refutation, noting that the objection does not apply to al-Nazzam's doctrine where both bodies and accidents are non-enduring. The passage concludes by discussing the views of al-Ash'ari and the Ash'aris on the endurance of accidents, noting a contradiction and stating the correct position.

The objection presented seems logically sound: if accidents are parts of the body, then the body should be temporary. Yet the author refuses to accept that conclusion because he knows the body endures. Consider an objection to a core belief of yours—perhaps about God’s attributes or human responsibility. Have you honestly confronted that objection, or do you avoid it because it feels threatening? What would it cost you to wrestle with it as al-Jurjani does?
?
Al-Jurjani insists that accidents are not included in the reality of substances. This distinction means that something essential (substance) is not defined by its accidental features. Think about your own identity: What accidental labels (career, status, wealth) do you cling to as if they were your essence? If they were stripped away, would you still know who you truly are? Answer not with a cliché, but with a concrete commitment to detach from one such accident this week.

Replies to the Objection and Doctrinal Positions
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection and analyzes various doctrinal stances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the objection and how different schools (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, al-Nazzam) address the composition of the body.

Core Idea
The body endures because its accidents are renewed, not fixed; this resolves the objection and highlights sectarian differences.

The objection raised in the previous lesson is now addressed. The author explains that the objection only holds if the accidents are considered fixed parts. If accidents are renewed, like the philosophers' matter-form relationship, the body can persist. He then examines the implications for those who affirm the homogeneity of substances, forcing them to include accidents in the body's definition. This leads to a discussion of the views of al-Nazzam, the Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari, revealing contradictions and clarifying the correct position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and follow the response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to how the author responds to the earlier objection.
The response uses the concept of renewal of accidents.
2
Understand the key point: renewal allows endurance
The author argues that the objection only applies if the part is a fixed set. If the accidents are renewed, the body can endure, analogous to matter enduring through the corporeal form.
This is a crucial distinction.
3
Notice the implication for homogeneous substances
The author asserts that those who affirm the homogeneity of substances must include accidents in the definition of body, otherwise bodies would be identical.
This ties the argument to broader kalam debates.
4
Identify the different schools' positions
The passage discusses al-Nazzam's view (bodies non-enduring), some Mu'tazila (deny similarity of substances), and al-Ash'ari (disparity of existents).
The author notes a contradiction in Ash'ari's view on endurance.
5
Extract the final resolution
The author concludes that the correct view is to affirm similarity of bodies and distinguish them by external attributes.
This aligns with the idea that the body is not a collection of accidents.

First Observatory - Second Purpose: The body is not a collection of assembled accidents. The sharing of substances in the mentioned attributes does not indicate their similarity in reality, because it is possible that those attributes are general accidents shared among their diverse realities. Hence we have no need then to include accidents in the realities of substances. (Therefore) i.e., because of their non-inclusion in them according to us, (we say:) Accidents do not endure, as previously mentioned, and substances endure, as will come. And it is not hidden that it can be made an objection by saying: Accidents are non-enduring, so they cannot be included in the enduring substances, because the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole. Know that there is no escape for one who acknowledges the homogeneity of individual substances and their similarity in reality—such as the Ash'aris collectively and most Mu'tazila—from including accidents in the reality of the body, so then the body is a substance with a set of accidents attached to that substance, because if it were composed solely of homogeneous substances, all bodies would be identical in essence, but rather they differ according to the accidents dependent on the will of the Chosen One. That difference is the doctrine of al-Nazzam, but some Mu'tazila do not affirm the similarity of substances, and the answer is completed according to their doctrine. And the claim that all bodies are similar by having the attributes of subsistence, occupying space, existing per se, accepting accidents, etc.—what bodies share and what is beyond that of caused attributes—is obstinacy. His saying: (to the inclusion of accidents and their composition from them). Complete on his saying: (If you make an objection) i.e., your proof, although it indicates that bodies are assembled accidents according to us, something negates it: if bodies were assembled accidents, they would be non-enduring because accidents are non-enduring and they are parts of bodies, and the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole. Then this objection does not apply to al-Nazzam according to what the commentator of al-Maqasid summarized by saying: bodies are also renewed, so the body according to them is etc. And the summary of the necessary non-endurance of bodies—since the negation of the part necessitates the negation of the whole—applies only if one says its inclusion is a specific set, not a particular set, but any set of similar renewed accidents, as the philosophers said in the endurance of matter through the corporeal form; otherwise no. His saying: (objection by saying etc.) In it, this objection is not valid according to al-Nazzam's doctrine because bodies are non-enduring according to him like accidents. And it can be said: the discussion is investigative, not obligating, and the endurance of bodies is necessary, so the opponent's non-acceptance does not harm; but there is something in it. His saying: (about including accidents in the reality of the body and about the lack of distinction between substances and accidents in renewal and endurance, since the renewal of the part necessitates the renewal of the whole, so one must turn to the fact that the one who denies the endurance of accidents is al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, and he does not affirm the similarity of substances, but rather existents according to him are diverse realities. As for the Ash'aris, they affirm their endurance. And you are aware that this contradicts what preceded in the investigations of accidents that al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers among the verifying Ash'aris affirm non-endurance. The truth is that the one who affirms the similarity of individual substances should choose the similarity of bodies, and that the distinction between them is by matters external to their reality.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument combined with objection-and-answer. The author first states the proposition: the body is not a collection of assembled accidents. He then argues that the fact that substances share certain attributes does not prove they are identical in reality, because those attributes could be general accidents common to diverse realities. Therefore, we do not need to include accidents in the essence of substances. The author then presents his own view: accidents do not endure (as previously established), while substances do endure. He anticipates an objection: if accidents are non-enduring, they cannot be part of enduring substances, because the removal of a part removes the whole. The author responds by noting that anyone who affirms the homogeneity of substances (like the Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila) must include accidents in the definition of body, otherwise bodies would all be identical. He then comments on al-Nazzam's position and the view of some Mu'tazila who deny the similarity of substances. The commentary further explains the objection and its refutation, noting that the objection does not apply to al-Nazzam's doctrine where both bodies and accidents are non-enduring. The passage concludes by discussing the views of al-Ash'ari and the Ash'aris on the endurance of accidents, noting a contradiction and stating the correct position.

Will you take time today to consciously attribute the persistence of your health, your mind, and your life to Allah's will at every instant?
?

Can you sincerely thank Allah for the specific 'accidents' that make you you, while trusting that He could renew them differently at any moment?

Mutual Entailment Does Not Imply Unity
Al-Jurjani refutes the second aspect of an argument for substance-accident unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary concomitance between substance and accident does not prove their unity or one being included in the other.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitance does not mean two things are one.

The objection attempts to prove that substance and accident are one because they always coexist and are mutually entailed. The answer uses a simple analogy from everyday language—correlative terms like father and son—to show this reasoning is flawed. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage once to grasp the structure: first the objection, then the answer. Notice the claim that mutual entailment in existence and non-existence is taken as evidence for unity.
2
Identify the author's response
The author denies that such entailment implies unity or inclusion. He gives the analogy of correlative terms (al-mutadā'ifayn). Think about examples like 'father' and 'son'—they imply each other but are distinct entities.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What is the mistake in the objection? Why might someone think coexistence proves identity? Then affirm that coexistence does not require oneness.
This connects to the learning objective.

الوجه ( الثاني : أنه إذا وجد الجسم بل الجوهر ( وجد الأعراض وإذا انتفى ) الجوهر انتفت وبالعكس) أي إذا وجدت الأعراض وجد الجوهر وإذا انتفت انتفى . ( قلنا : التلازم بينهما وجوداً وعدماً (لا يفيد الوحدة ولا دخول أحدهما في الآخر) كالمتضايفين.
The second aspect: that if the body, rather the substance, exists, accidents exist; and if the substance is negated, they are negated, and vice versa — i.e., if accidents exist, the substance exists, and if they are negated, it is negated. (We say:) The mutual entailment between them in existence and non-existence (does not imply unity nor the inclusion of one in the other), like correlative terms.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (the second aspect) and its answer. The objection argues that if the body or substance exists, accidents exist, and if the substance is negated, accidents are negated, and vice versa. This mutual entailment in existence and non-existence is taken by some to imply that substance and accident are united or that one is included in the other. The author responds that such mutual entailment does not imply unity or inclusion, using the analogy of correlative terms (al-mutadā'ifayn) such as father and son, which necessarily exist together but are not one entity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual entailment (always existing together) implies that two things are identical or one contains the other.
Correction: Mutual entailment only shows a necessary relationship, not identity. Like father and son, they are distinct but require each other.

Key Takeaway
Mutual entailment does not prove that two things are identical; like father and son, they coexist but remain distinct.

The Four Rational Possibilities for Simple Body Parts
Al-Jurjani classifies the theoretical possibilities for the parts of a simple body and details the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani classifies the rational possibilities for the parts of a simple body, and you will grasp the first possibility: the atomic theory of the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
The parts of a simple body may be actual or potential, finite or infinite — four possibilities.

This lesson begins the discussion of simple bodies. After distinguishing composite bodies, al-Jurjani states that simple bodies do accept division conceptually. He then lays out four theoretical possibilities based on whether the assumed parts exist actually and whether they are finite or infinite. Only the first possibility is detailed here, representing the atomic view of the majority of kalam scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Jurjani sets up the problem: first distinguishing composite from simple, then stating that simple bodies accept division, then posing two binary questions (actual vs. non-actual, finite vs. infinite) that yield four possibilities.
Only the first possibility is fully explained; the lesson focuses on that.
2
Identify the four possibilities
From the passage, list the four theoretical possibilities. The text explicitly names them as numbers one through four, but only the first is described. Write them down: 1) all parts exist actually and finite, 2) all parts exist actually and infinite, 3) not all parts exist actually and finite, 4) not all parts exist actually and infinite.
This classification is purely rational; each possibility corresponds to a school.
3
Understand the first possibility in depth
Study the explanation for the first possibility: the parts that can be assumed all exist actually and are finite. This is the doctrine of the majority of mutakallimun, affirming indivisible atoms. The author explains why if parts were divisible, not all possible divisions would be actual.
The argument ties actuality and finitude together.
4
Reflect on the kalam context
Ask yourself: Why would the mutakallimun insist on finite actual parts? How does this relate to the broader Islamic theological project of defending creation and divine power? This will be explored further in later lessons.

Third Purpose: The simple body accepts division. The body is either composite of bodies with different realities—then there is no doubt that its different parts exist in it actually and are finite, like an animal—or it is simple, which is not like that, such as water. The dispute is only about the latter. So we say: The simple body undoubtedly accepts division and partition, such that something other than something is assumed in it. Then the parts that can be assumed either all exist actually or do not exist so; and in either case they are either finite or infinite. So the rational possibilities are four: First: The parts that can be assumed all exist actually and are finite, which is the doctrine of the majority of the mutakallimun, namely the statement that it is composed of parts that are indivisible at all—ne by cutting due to smallness, nor by breaking due to hardness, nor by imagination due to the inability of the imagination to distinguish one extremity from another, nor by rational assumption. And we only say that it is the statement that it is composed of those parts (because if the parts were divisible—i.e., capable of division even hypothetically—then the possible divisions would not all be actually present, so the parts that can be assumed would not all exist in it actually, contrary to the supposition). Its summary is that our statement: All divisions that are possible are actually present (which is the meaning of our statement: all possible parts according to assumption exist actually) entails our statement: Every division that is not actually present is not…
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the rational possibilities regarding the composition of a simple body. The author first distinguishes composite bodies (like animals) from simple bodies (like water). The dispute is about simple bodies. He then states that a simple body certainly accepts division in the sense that we can assume different parts within it. The key question is whether all possible parts exist actually, and whether they are finite or infinite. This yields four rational possibilities, but only the first is detailed here: that all possible parts exist actually and are finite. This is the doctrine of the majority of mutakallimun, who affirm indivisible parts (atoms). The author explains that if parts were divisible, not all possible divisions could be actual, contradicting the assumption. The passage ends abruptly, so only the first possibility is covered.

The Simple Body and Its Seven Possible Divisions
Defines the subject and lists seven possible divisions of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the simple body in kalam and the seven rational possibilities for its division, along with the realization that only one corresponds to an actual doctrine.

Core Idea
The simple body can be divided in seven ways, but only one has been adopted as a doctrine.

Sanusi begins the fourth aim by defining its subject: the simple body, an indivisible unit in atomism. He then considers an objection that might lead to multiple division possibilities. The passage clarifies that while there are seven rational possibilities, only one is actually held as a doctrine. This sets the stage for discussing atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the structure: an objection, an enumeration, and a reply.
2
Identify the subject of investigation
The passage states the subject is the 'simple body' (الجسم المفرد). Understand what this means: a body not composed of smaller bodies.
This is a key term in atomism.
3
Examine the objection and seven possibilities
The objection raises the possibility of multiple division types. The text lists seven possibilities outside the four known doctrines. Count: the enumeration mentions all directions, two directions, one direction, and mixed—these yield seven specific possibilities.
The exact breakdown of the seven is not detailed, but the key is that there are seven.
4
Understand the reply
Sanusi accepts the logic but notes that six of these possibilities have no supporters—they are merely rational possibilities, not actual doctrines.
This shows the difference between logical possibility and historical belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث هو الجسم المفرد وهو الذي لا يتركب من أجزاء هي أجسام كان مذهبه خارجاء عنه، فإن قلت : إذا كان بعض الانقسامات حاصلاً دون بعض احتمل أن تكون أجزاء الجسم الموجودة فيه بالفعل المتصلة في أنفسها قابلة للانقسام في الجهات كلها أو في جهتين أو في جهة واحدة أو مختلطة منها، فهذه احتمالات سبعة خارجة عن المذاهب الأربعة، قلت: هذا صحيح، إلا أن ستة منها لم يذهب إليها أحد، فهي احتمالات عقلية لا مذاهب .
The investigation is the simple body, which does not compose from parts that are bodies; his doctrine is outside of it. If you say: if some divisions occur without others, it is possible that the parts of the body existing in it actually, connected in themselves, are divisible in all directions, or in two directions, or in one direction, or mixed from them; these are seven possibilities outside the four doctrines. I reply: this is correct, except that six of them have not been adopted by anyone; they are rational possibilities, not doctrines.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines the subject of the fourth aim and enumerates possible divisions of bodies. It begins by stating that the investigation concerns the simple body (al-jism al-mufrad), which does not contain parts that are themselves bodies. The author then presents an objection: if some divisions are possible and others not, then the actual parts of a body could be divisible in all directions, two directions, one direction, or mixed, giving seven possibilities beyond the four known doctrines. The author concedes that these are indeed possibilities, but notes that six have no adherents; they are merely rational possibilities, not actual doctrines. Thus the passage establishes the framework for discussing atomism.

الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All seven division possibilities are actual doctrines held by some scholars.
Correction: The author explicitly states that six of the seven possibilities have no adherents; they are only rational possibilities, not doctrines.

A) Four

Key Takeaway
The simple body is the subject of investigation, and although seven divisions are logically possible, only one corresponds to an actual doctrine—the rest are mere rational possibilities.

The First Argument: Division of Unity
Presents the first argument (division of unity) for the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument of the mutakallimun that every divisible thing must have actual parts, based on the impossibility of unity being divided.

Core Idea
If a divisible thing were truly one, its indivisible unity would have to divide—an impossibility.

The mutakallimun begin their proof for actual parts by assuming the opposite: what if a divisible thing were a single undivided unity? They then show this leads to a contradiction: the unity inhering in it would have to split, but unity by definition cannot split. This lesson unpacks that argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional claim
Recognize the assumption: if a divisible thing were one and not actually divided, then unity would have to divide because division of the place necessitates division of what inheres.
The 'place' here is the divisible thing itself; the 'inhering' is unity.
2
See why the consequent is false
Unity by definition is indivisible; there is no meaning to unity except not being divided. So the consequent (division of unity) is impossible.
This is a logical contradiction, so the assumption must be false.
3
Conclude the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to absurdity, the opposite must be true: the divisible thing already has actual parts, because if it were truly one, it could not be divided without absurdity.
Thus the first argument establishes that 'everything capable of division is actually divided.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: حجة المتكلمين على التقسيم. في حجة جمهور (المتكلمين) على مذهبهم ( وهي نوعان : النوع الأول أن نبين أولاً أن كل منقسم أي قابل للانقسام ( له أجزاء بالفعل ) ثم نبين أنها ) أي تلك الانقسامات والأجزاء الحاصلة بالفعل ( متناهية ) فيعلم من الأول أن أجزاء الجسم البسيط حاصلة بالفعل غير قابلة للانقسام ومن الثاني تناهيها ( أما الأول : ( وهو أن كل ما يقبل القسمة فهو منقسم بالفعل (فلوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : القابل للقسمة لو كان واحداً) في نفسه غير منقسم بالفعل ( لزم انقسام الوحدة والتالي ،باطل، فالشرطية ) أي استلزام المقدم للتالي، لأنه يلزم على ذلك التقدير ( قيام الوحدة ) الحقيقية (بما يقبل القسمة وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال فيه ضرورة أن الحال في أحد الجزأين غير الحال في الجزء الآخر، والاستثنائية ) أي بطلان التالي ( بينة إذ لا معنى للوحدة إلا كونها لا تنقسم . الوجه ( الثاني : لو كان القابل للانقسام واحدا في نفسه متصلا في حد ذاته ( كان التفريق ( الوارد على ذلك القابل ( إعداما له ) وإيجادا متصلة لغيره ) والتالي باطل. أما الملازمة فلأن التفريق حينئذ إعدام لهوية ) هي حد ذاتها ( وإحداث لهويتين منفصلتين لم تكونا موجودتين في تلك الهوية الاتصالية وإلا كانت منقسمة بالفعل والمفروض خلافه. وقد وجب كون التفريق على ذلك التقدير إعداماً وإحداثاً.
The fourth aim: The proof of the mutakallimun on division. On the proof of the majority of the mutakallimun for their doctrine, and it is of two types: The first type is that we first show that every divisible, i.e., capable of division, has actual parts, then we show that they are finite. So from the first it is known that the parts of the simple body are actual and not capable of division, and from the second their finitude. As for the first: that everything capable of division is actually divided; it has three aspects. First: if the divisible were one in itself not actually divided, division of unity would follow, and the consequent is false. The conditional [claim] is that real unity subsists in what is capable of division, and division of the place necessitates division of what inheres in it because what inheres in one part is not what inheres in another part. The exceptional [claim]—that the consequent is false—is evident since there is no meaning to unity except its being indivisible. Second: If the divisible were one in itself continuous in its essence, the separation that occurs to that divisible would be a destruction of it and a bringing into existence of a continuous thing other than it; and the consequent is false. As for the implication, because separation then is a destruction of an essence that is its very self and a generation of two separate essences that were not existing in that continuous essence, otherwise it would be actually divided, contrary to the assumption. And it has been necessary that separation in that estimation is destruction and generation.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the first part of the mutakallimun's proof for their doctrine of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The proof is of two types; here we have the first type, which aims to establish two things: first, that every divisible thing (qābil li-l-inqisām) actually has parts (ajzāʼ bi-l-fiʻl), and second, that those parts are finite (mutanāhiya). The first claim is argued with three aspects (wujūh). The first aspect is that if the divisible were one and not actually divided, then the unity (al-waḥda) inhering in it would have to divide, since the division of the place (al-maḥall) necessitates division of what inheres in it (al-ḥāl). The consequence is false because unity by definition is indivisible. The second aspect is that if the divisible were one and continuous in its essence, then any separation (tafrīq) applied to it would amount to destroying that essence and generating two separate essences, which is impossible. Thus the assumption that it is one and undivided leads to absurdity, proving that it must already have actual parts. The text gives the first two of three arguments, with detailed reasoning.

Key Terms
القابل للقسمة
الوحدة
انقسام المحل
الحال
الشرطية
الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'unity' here refers to the number one or a mathematical unit, implying that dividing the thing would simply break it into smaller numbers, which is not impossible.
Correction: The passage defines unity as 'that which is not divided' (lā tanqasim). It is an attribute of indivisibility, not a quantity. The argument is that if the thing were truly indivisible (one), any division would destroy that attribute, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint
In the first argument, what does the author say would necessarily happen if a divisible thing were one and not actually divided?
A) The thing would cease to exist
B) Unity would divide
C) The parts would become infinite
D) The thing would become continuous

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that assuming a divisible thing is a single undivided unity forces a contradiction, proving that it must already have actual parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun proved that unity cannot be divided without contradiction. Where in your life are you trying to divide your allegiance between Allah and something else? How does this logical truth expose the impossibility of a divided heart?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's oneness is absolute and indivisible, what one area of your life must you surrender to Him today that you have been holding back?
?
The argument shows that a thing's state cannot be split from its reality. When you profess tawhid with your tongue but your actions contradict it, are you not living a contradiction similar to the one the proof refutes? What is one step you will take this week to make your actions match your profession?
