Commentary
This passage builds on the previous classification by introducing factors that can prevent physical (infikākiyya) division, such as substantial form, hardness, lack of tools, or extreme smallness. It then provides a more refined logical classification: division either leads to actual separation (the disintegrative type) or it does not. ?

Two Explanatory Notes: Accidental Differences and Substantial Form
Glosses explaining accidental differences and substantial form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blackness and whiteness cannot occupy the same locus and how substantial form distinguishes one substance from another.

Core Idea
Accidental differences and substantial form define the boundaries of individual things.

This short passage consists of two glosses that clarify key terms in Kalam discussions of composition. The first explains why two contrary accidents cannot share a single locus, while the second distinguishes the substantial form that makes a thing what it is from the mere corporeal form. These examples ground abstract principles in concrete cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on blackness and whiteness
Start by reading the first Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the two reasons given for why the loci of blackness and whiteness are different.
The reasons are: one accident cannot have two loci, and two contraries cannot coexist.
2
Understand the principle of accidental differences
Reflect on how this principle applies to all accidental qualities: each accident must have its own distinct locus, and contrary accidents cannot be in the same place at the same time.
3
Read the gloss on substantial form
Move to the second sentence. Note the distinction between substantial form (which gives a thing its essential identity) and corporeal form (which only gives extension).
The water example illustrates how reaching a specific limit triggers substantial form.
4
Grasp the role of substantial form in defining substance
Understand that each element has a specific limit; when matter reaches that limit, the substantial form is realized and the substance exists. This applies to all elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (كالسواد والبياض) فإن محل السواد مغاير في الخارج بمحل البياض؛ لامتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين ولامتناع اجتماع الضدين، تدبر. قوله: (كصورة نوعية) وأما الصورة الجسمية فغير مانعة عن قبول التجريد، فعلى هذا لعنصر الماء حد معين، إذا وصل إليه تحقق الصورة النوعية وصار ماء، وإذا لم يصل إليه انتفى الماء، وكذا سائر العناصر.
His saying: 'like blackness and whiteness' — for the locus of blackness is different in external existence from the locus of whiteness, due to the impossibility of one accidental quality subsisting in two loci and the impossibility of two contraries coexisting. Ponder. His saying: 'like a substantial form' — and as for the corporeal form, it does not prevent the acceptance of abstraction. So on this, the element water has a specific limit: when it reaches it, the substantial form is realized and it becomes water; when it does not reach it, water ceases to exist, and likewise the rest of the elements.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage consists of two explanatory notes on terms used in the main text. The first note explains the example of blackness and whiteness: these are contrary accidental qualities that cannot occupy the same locus at the same time; hence, if they are present in different parts of a body, those parts are distinguished. The second note explains the term 'substantial form' (al-ṣūra al-nawʿiyya) as opposed to 'corporeal form' (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya). The corporeal form does not prevent abstraction or division, but the substantial form determines the identity of a substance. For instance, the element water has a specific material limit; when matter attains that limit, it acquires the substantial form of water and becomes water; otherwise, it is not water. This applies to all elements.

Key Terms
عرض
سواد
بياض
صورة نوعية
صورة جسمية
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any difference in properties between two parts of a body implies they are separate substances.
Correction: The note explains that the separation is due to the impossibility of two contrary accidents sharing the same locus, not merely any difference.

Checkpoint
Why does the text say the locus of blackness is different from the locus of whiteness?
A) Because black and white are colors.
B) Because one accident cannot subsist in two loci and two contraries cannot coexist.
C) Because blackness is darker.
D) Because they are abstract concepts.

Key Takeaway
Accidental differences and substantial form are two key concepts that explain how individual things are distinguished from one another in Kalam ontology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that water only becomes water when it reaches its specific limit (ḥadd). Consider the 'limits' Allah has set for your own life—your abilities, your circumstances, your time. Have you ever, like impure matter, resisted reaching the limit that would allow you to become what He intends? What would change if you fully surrendered to that limit?
?
The impossibility of two contraries coexisting in one locus is a physical principle. Yet we often try to hold contradictory intentions in our hearts—wanting both the world and the Hereafter, comfort and sacrifice. Where in your life are you trying to keep two contraries together? Will you confess this to Allah and ask Him to clarify and purify your intention?
?
When the author says 'ponder' (tadabbar), he invites you to reflect deeply. What is one thing in your life that you have understood only superficially, like someone mistaking corporeal form for substantial form? What step will you take today to go deeper and grasp its true essence in the light of this lesson?

The Simple Body is Not Composed of Indivisible Parts
Proves that simple body lacks indivisible parts via continuity and infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a simple body like water is not composed of indivisible parts, and why infinite divisibility is necessary.

Core Idea
A simple body is continuous and divisible without end; indivisible parts would contradict sensation.

The author begins by stating the thesis that the simple body is not composed of indivisible parts. He provides a proof based on sensory continuity and infinite divisibility. This lesson walks through that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
Read the opening sentence: the simple body is not divisible into indivisible parts. Notice how the author sets up the claim.
The thesis is presented as the title of the section.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is that the body is perceived as one continuous thing and is divisible without end. If it were composed of indivisible parts, division would eventually reach those parts and stop, which contradicts our experience.
The key is the conditional: 'otherwise the existence of the part would be necessary at the end of division.'
3
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is that the body is not actually composed of indivisible parts; it is continuous in itself and capable of infinite divisions.
Note that 'infinite' here means potentially infinite, not actually infinite in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : الجسم البسيط لا يتجزأ بالبرهان أن الجسم البسيط كالماء مثلاً لا ينفصل إلى أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وما في حكمها من الجواهر المنقسمة في جهة واحدة أو في جهتين فقط، فقد ثبت أنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة لا مفصل فيه أصلا، كما هو عند الحس وقابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية أي لا تصل قسمته إلى حد تقف عنده كما مر، وإلا لزم وجود الجزء عند انتهاء القسمة والحاصل أن ذلك الجسم ليس مركباً بالفعل من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وما في حكمها فيكون متصلاً في نفسه ولا تنتهي قسمته إليها، فيكون قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية والقسمة إما بالفك كسراً أو قطعاً، والفرق بينهما أن القطع يحتاج إلى آلة نفاذة فاصلة بالنفوذ دون الكسر وأيضاً للقطع نوع اختصاص بالأجسام اللينة والكسر بالأجسام الصلبة، وإما باختلاف عرضين قارين في محلهما لا بالقياس إلى غيره كالسواد والبياض أو غير قارين في المحل باعتبار نفسه بل بالإضافة إلى غيره كمماستين ومحاذاتين وإما بالوهم والفرض، فهذه الثلاثة وجوه القسمة في الجسم. نعم قد يمنع عن القسمة الانفكاكية مانع كصورة نوعية كما في الأفلاك أو صلابة شديدة في بعض الأجسام العنصرية، أو فقد آلة يحتاج إليها في القطع أو صغر متبالغ لا يعتبر معه القطع ولا الكسر وأما القسمة الفرضية فلا تقف أبداً وقد بين انحصار القسمة في الثلاثة المذكورة بأنها إما مؤدية إلى الافتراق وهي الفكية أو لا وحينئذ إما أن تكون موجبة للانفصال في الخارج وهي التي باختلاف عرضين أو في الذهن وهي الوهمية . وإنما ذكر الفرض العقلي مع الوهم لأن الوهم ربما لم يقدر على تمييز طرف عن طرف لغاية الصغر فيقف بخلاف العقل فإنه لا يقف لإحاطته بالكليات المشتملة على الكبير والصغير. والصواب : إن اختلاف الأعراض لا يوجب انفصالاً خارجياً لأنا نعلم قطعاً أن الجسم المتصل في نفسه إذا وقع ضوء على بعضه لم ينفصل في الخارج حتى إذا زال الضوء عنه عاد إلى اتصاله بل هذا الاختلاف باعث للوهم على فرض الأجزاء، وحينئذ يقال : الانفصال إما في الخارج كما بالقطع والكسر وإما في الوهم. قوله : كالسواد والبياض فإن محل السواد مغاير في الخارج بمحل البياض ؛ لامتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين ولامتناع اجتماع الضدين، تدبر. قوله : كصورة نوعية وأما الصورة الجسمية فغير مانعة عن قبول التجريد فعلى هذا لعنصر الماء حد معين إذا وصل إليه تحقق الصورة النوعية وصار ماء وإذا لم يصل إليه انتفى الماء وكذا سائر العناصر. انفكاكية وهي قسمة خارجية منقسمة إلى قسميها، وغير قسمة ذهنية وتسمى وهمية وفرضية أيضاً وتنقسم إلى القسمين المذكورين هذا هو الضبط وقد يفرق بين الفرضية والوهمية كما أشرنا إليه ويجعل ما باختلاف الأعراض قسيماً للوهمية المجردة كما في الكتاب فعليك بالتثبت في موارد الاستعمال.
First Observation - Sixth Section: The simple body is not divisible into parts that are not divisible [indivisible parts], and what is in the same ruling of the atoms divided in one dimension or two dimensions only. It has been established that it is one continuous [entity] in reality, having no division at all, as it is [perceived] by sensation, and it is divisible without end, meaning its division does not reach a limit at which it stops, as has passed; otherwise the existence of the [indivisible] part would be necessary at the end of division. The conclusion is that that body is not actually composed of indivisible parts and what is in their ruling, so it is continuous in itself and its division does not end at them, thus it is capable of infinite divisions. Division is either by separation: breaking or cutting; the difference between them is that cutting needs a penetrating separating instrument, while breaking does not. Also, cutting has a specific appropriateness to soft bodies, and breaking to hard bodies. [Division] either by the difference of two permanent accidents in their locus, not in relation to something else, like blackness and whiteness; or non-permanent [accidents] in the locus considered by itself but with relation to something else, like two contacts and two adjacencies. Or by estimation and hypothesis. These are the three modes of division in a body. Indeed, a preventing factor may prevent separative division, such as a specific form as in the celestial spheres, or extreme hardness in some elemental bodies, or the lack of an instrument needed for cutting, or extreme smallness whereby neither cutting nor breaking is considered. As for hypothetical division, it never stops. And it has been explained that division is confined to the three mentioned: either leading to separation, which is separative; or not, then either causing separation in externality, which is by difference of accidents, or in the mind, which is estimative. He only mentioned intellectual hypothesis along with estimation because estimation perhaps cannot distinguish one side from another due to extreme smallness, so it stops, unlike the intellect, which does not stop due to its encompassing the universals that include large and small. The correct view: difference of accidents does not cause external separation, for we know certainly that a body continuous in itself, when light falls on some part of it, does not separate externally until when the light goes away it returns to its continuity. Rather, this difference incites estimation to hypothesize parts. Hence it is said: separation is either externally, as by cutting and breaking, or in estimation. His statement: 'like blackness and whiteness'—for the locus of blackness is different externally from the locus of whiteness, due to the impossibility of one accident inhering in two loci, and the impossibility of two opposites combining. Reflect. His statement: 'like a specific form'—as for corporeal form, it does not prevent acceptance of abstraction. Thus, the water element has a defined limit; when it reaches it the specific form is realized and it becomes water, and when it does not reach it, the water ceases to exist, and similarly for other elements. Separative [division] is an external division divided into its two parts, and non-mental division is called estimative and hypothetical as well, and it is divided into the two mentioned divisions. This is the regulation, and one may distinguish between hypothetical and estimative as we indicated, and he makes what is by difference of accidents a counterpart of pure estimative division as in the book. So you must verify in the contexts of usage.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification. The author (al-Jurjani) begins by stating the thesis: the simple body (like water) is not composed of indivisible parts. The proof: it is perceived as a continuous unity and is divisible without end; if it were composed of indivisible parts, division would stop at those parts, but we observe it can be divided indefinitely. Therefore it is not actually composed of indivisible parts. He then classifies division into three types: 1) by physical separation (breaking or cutting), 2) by difference of accidents (like color differences) which may suggest separation, and 3) by estimation or hypothesis (mental division). He explains the difference between breaking and cutting, and notes that certain factors like specific forms, hardness, or smallness can prevent physical separation, but hypothetical division never stops. He clarifies that division based on accident differences does not cause actual external separation; it is merely an estimative suggestion. He concludes by summarizing that division is either external (separative) or mental (estimative/hypothetical), and one should be careful in how these terms are used.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
قسمة إلى غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if bodies are divisible without end, there must be actual indivisible parts at some point.
Correction: The passage argues that division is potential, not actual infinite, and indivisible parts would contradict the observed continuity of bodies.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion about the simple body?
A) It is composed of indivisible parts.
B) It is continuous and divisible without end.
C) It is composed of atoms.
D) It cannot be divided.

Key Takeaway
The simple body is not actually made of indivisible parts; it is infinitely divisible potentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that a simple body is not made of discrete parts but is continuous and infinitely divisible. Your own body is made of cells, molecules, atoms—each further divisible. Does this remind you that everything in creation points to a Creator who alone is indivisible and infinite? How will you let this truth humble your heart today and deepen your reliance on Allah?
?
The proof relies on sensation and reason working together. When have you allowed your sensory desires or immediate feelings to override sound reasoning and divine revelation? What specific step can you take this week to prioritize revelation over impulse?
?
If division can go on without end, then material things have no ultimate fixed unit. Your possessions, your status, even your body are not solid, independent realities. How does this challenge your attachment to worldly things? What is one attachment you will consciously loosen starting today?

The Three Types of Division in Bodies
Classifies division into three types and discusses preventing factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of division in bodies: separative, by accident difference, and hypothetical, along with their subtypes and limitations.

Core Idea
Division is either physical separative, by accident difference, or mental hypothetical.

Having established the continuity of simple bodies, the author now classifies division into three types: breaking/cutting, difference of accidents, and estimation/hypothesis. This lesson explains each type and their characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of division
Read the opening sentence: 'Division is either by separation: breaking or cutting; or by difference of accidents; or by estimation and hypothesis.' Notice the three main categories.
2
Understand separative division
Separative division includes breaking and cutting. The author explains their differences: cutting requires an instrument and is for soft bodies; breaking is for hard bodies without an instrument.
Note the factors that can prevent separative division, like specific forms or extreme smallness.
3
Understand division by accident differences
Division can also be conceived by difference in accidents like blackness and whiteness. However, the author clarifies this does not cause actual separation; it only suggests division to the mind.
The example of light falling on part of a body shows it remains continuous.
4
Understand hypothetical division
Hypothetical division is mental and never stops. Estimation may stop due to smallness, but the intellect can continue indefinitely.
This type is unlimited and always possible.

?

?

The Body Is Composed of Hylè and Form
Proof that body is composed of hylè and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that a simple body must be composed of a material substrate (hylè) and a corporeal form, and why the corporeal form is not an accident but an extended substance.

Core Idea
A simple body is continuous yet divisible, revealing its composition from hylè and form.

This lesson presents the seventh section of the discussion, which provides a rational proof that the body is not a simple substance but is composed of two principles: hylè (matter) and corporeal form. The argument draws on the observable fact that a simple body is continuous in itself yet capable of separation, implying that there is an extended substance (corporeal form) that inheres in a receptive substrate (hylè). As you read, note how the proof distinguishes the form from the underlying matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the core proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the opening claim and the argument that follows.
The passage uses technical terms; note them for later reference.
2
Identify the two premises
Identify the two observations about the body: it is continuous (متصل) and it is capable of separation (قابل للانفصال). How do these lead to the conclusion?
3
Understand the nature of corporeal form
The passage calls the continuous substance 'corporeal form' (صورة جسمية). It emphasizes that this form is not the entire reality of the body—it inheres in something else.
Contrast this with the idea of form as an accident; the passage clarifies it is a substance.
4
Grasp the role of hylè
There must be another thing (أمر آخر) that sustains the continuity. This is the hylè or material substrate. Understand why it is necessary.
Hylè is not directly observed but inferred from the body's divisibility.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Consider how the proof moves from observable features to an unobserved composition. This is a typical kalam argument from contingency.
The argument is a posteriori, based on empirical qualities.

المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة دليلهم على إثبات الهيولى والصورة وكون الجسم مركباً منهما، قالوا: فالجسم البسيط متصل واحد في حد ذاته كما عرفت وهو قابل للانفصال الانفكاكي، كما إذا صب ماء الجرة في إنائين، فثمة اتصال أي جوهر ممتد في الجهات متصل في نفسه نسميه الصورة الجسمية وندعي أنه أي ذلك الجوهر المتصل ليس بتمام حقيقة الجسم بل ثمة أمر آخر يقوم به الاتصال أي الجوهر المتصل على معنى أنه يختص به اختصاصاً ناعتاً له فيكون حالاً فيه. وبيانه أن. قوله : فالجسم إلخ أي إذا تقرر نفي الجزء وما في حكمه فالجسم البسيط أي الذي لا يتألف من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع متصل في حد ذاته أي لا مفصل فيه كما هو عند الحس. قوله : وهو قابل للانفصال أي يتصف بالانفصال في الجملة سواء كان باعتبار نفسه أو باعتبار جزئه. قوله: فثمة اتصال إلخ في شرح الإشارات الاتصال يدل على معنيين: أحدهما صفة الشيء لا بقياسه وهو كونه بحيث يمكن له أجزاء مشترك في الحدود والمتصل بهذا المعنى يطلق على فصل الكم على الصورة الجسمية المستلزمة للجسم التعليمي عند ما يطلق المتصل على الصورة الجسمية اتصال أيضاً، وقد يقال لهذه الصورة أيضاً اتصال وامتداد لمجاورته ويقال : للجسم بحسب ذلك متصل انتهى . فهي جوهر متصل في نفسه واتصال بالقياس إلى أن الجسم متصل بها . قوله : نسميه الصورة لأن الجسم إنما صار جسماً بها . قوله : على معنى إلخ لا على أنه مقوم له كقيام العرض بالمحل . قوله : وقد يفرق بين الفرضية والوهمية كما أشرنا إليه من قولنا : وإنما ذكر الفرض العقلي إلخ، ويجعل ما بالاختلاف قسماً للوهمية المجردة. قوله : أي جوهر ممتد أشار إلى أن المراد بالاتصال هاهنا الجوهر الممتد المتصل في ذاته فإنه يسمى في الاصطلاح تارة اتصالاً وتارة انفصالاً مبالغة في كونه ممتداً متصلاً في ذاته .
The Seventh Section: The body is composed of hylè and form. Their proof for affirming hylè and form and that the body is composed of them: they said: so the simple body is one continuous [entity] in itself as you have known, and it is capable of separative separation, as when water from a jar is poured into two vessels. There, there is continuity, i.e., an extended substance in directions continuous in itself, which we call corporeal form; and we claim that it – i.e., that continuous substance – is not the entire reality of the body, but there is another thing that sustains the continuity, i.e., the continuous substance, in the sense that it is particularized by it as a describing particularization, so it is a state in it. And its explanation is that... His statement: 'so the body etc.' meaning: when the negation of the indivisible part and what is in its ruling has been established, the simple body – i.e., that which is not composed of bodies of different natures – is continuous in itself, i.e., having no internal division, as it is according to sensation. His statement: 'and it is capable of separation' i.e., it is characterized by separation in general, whether considered by itself or by its parts. His statement: 'there, there is continuity etc.' In Sharh al-Isharat, continuity indicates two meanings: one is the attribute of a thing not by comparison to it, i.e., its being such that it can have parts sharing boundaries; the continuous in this sense is applied to the category of quantity, to the corporeal form that entails the teaching body, and the continuous is also said of the corporeal form as continuity. And this form may also be called continuity and extension due to adjacency, and the body is accordingly called continuous. End quote. So it is a substance continuous in itself, and continuity in relation to the body being continuous by it. His statement: 'we call it form' because the body only becomes a body by it. His statement: 'in the sense that etc.' not in that it constitutes it like the inherence of an accident in a substrate. His statement: 'and one may distinguish between hypothetical and estimative as we indicated' in our saying: 'and He only mentioned intellectual hypothesis etc., and he makes what is by difference of accidents a counterpart of pure estimative division.' His statement: 'i.e., an extended substance' indicates that what is meant by continuity here is the extended substance continuous in itself, for it is called sometimes continuity and sometimes extension in exaggeration of its being extended and continuous in itself.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the composition of the body from hylè (matter) and form (corporeal form). The argument: we observe that a simple body is continuous (extended) in itself yet also capable of separation (e.g., pouring water into two vessels). This implies that there is some continuous substrate (an extended substance) which we call corporeal form. However, this continuous substance is not the entire reality of the body; there must be another underlying principle (hylè) that supports the continuity. In other words, the body's being divisible shows it has two aspects: the form (corporeal form) which makes it extended and continuous, and the hylè which is the substrate that receives this form. The commentary emphasizes that 'corporeal form' is the extended substance itself, but it is not the complete reality; the hylè is distinct. The commentary also distinguishes between hypothetical and estimative division, referencing earlier discussion. The key point is that the body is composite: it has a material substrate (hylè) and a formal principle (corporeal form).

صورة جسمية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The corporeal form is an accident that gives shape to the body.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the corporeal form is a substance (جوهر ممتد) that inheres in the hylè as a qualifying attribute, not like an accident in a substrate. It is the principle of extension itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the corporeal form?
A) An accident that gives the body its shape
B) The extended continuous substance that makes the body a body
C) The hylè or material substrate
D) The specific form of each element

Key Takeaway
The body is not simple; it is composed of a receptive hylè and an extended corporeal form, proven by its simultaneous continuity and divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage uses the example of pouring water into two vessels to show that the same body can be divided while retaining its identity. When you think about the changes your body undergoes (growth, illness, aging), does your sense of self remain anchored in something deeper than the physical? How can you train yourself to see your body's continuity as a constant sign of Allah's sustaining power?
?

Are you more concerned with the form that will perish or the underlying substance that Allah will resurrect? What step can you take today to prioritize the reality of your soul over the form of your body?

Ibn Sina's Argument Against Indivisible Parts
Ibn Sina refutes atomism by showing homogeneous atoms must be divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's logical argument that homogeneous indivisible parts cannot be truly indivisible because they share an essence that admits separation and connection.

Core Idea
If atoms share the same essence, they must share the same properties including divisibility.

The doctrine of atomism posits that bodies are composed of tiny indivisible parts. Ibn Sina challenges this by imagining the imaginary division of one such part. If all atoms are identical in essence, the two halves produced by this mental division must have the same nature as any two separate atoms. Therefore, they must be capable of actual separation, contradicting indivisibility. This lesson unpacks Ibn Sina's rigorous argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, focusing on the flow of the argument. Pay attention to the key terms: جزء لا يتجزأ, انقسام وهمي, ماهية.
The passage is from a commentary, so it includes explanatory remarks.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
Ibn Sina's argument has a clear logical flow: it presents the opponent's view, then shows that homogeneity forces a contradiction: two parts of the same atom must be able to separate.
Look for the phrases 'فلم لا يجوز' (first objection) and 'وأبطله ابن سينا' (refutation).
3
Analyze the key premise
The argument rests on the assumption that the small bodies (atoms) are concordant in specific essence. Understand why this is crucial: if they share essence, they share all concomitant properties.
The commentary clarifies that this assumption holds only for homogeneous bodies like water.
4
Consider the exception
The argument ends with 'unless there is an external impediment.' But the commentator shows that such an impediment cannot be a necessary property of the atom's essence.
This closes the loophole, making the refutation universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انتفاء الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ وما في حكمه يستلزم أن الجسم إما أن يكون متصلا في نفسه فيكون جسماً مفرداً، أو يكون في تركيبه منتهياً إلى أجسام مفردة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الجسم البسيط الذي نحن بصدده مركباً من أجسام مفردة قابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون الفكي فلا تثبت الهيولى بالبرهان المذكور لابتنائه على أن الجسم المتصل في نفسه يرد عليه الانفصال الخارجي بل ولا يثبت أيضاً الجسم التعليمي، لأن تلك الأجسام المفردة لا تتغير أشكالها ومقاديرها، ( وأبطله ) أي قول هذا القائل ( ابن سينا بما حاصله أن كل جزء منها أي من تلك الأجزاء القابلة للانقسام الوهمي ( تحدث فيه القسمة الوهمية اثنينية يكون طباع كل منهما طباع الآخر) وطباع الجملة وهو ظاهر ( و) طباع الجزء الآخر، (الخارج الموافق لها في الماهية ) بناء على ما ذهب إليه ذلك القائل من أن تلك الأجسام المفردة الصغار متوافقة في الماهية النوعية ( فيجوز) حينئذ ( على ) الجزأين ( المتصلين) المفروضين في جزء واحد (ما يجوز على ( الجزأين ( المنفصلين ) أعني الجزء الذي قسم والجزء الآخر ( من الانفصال ) الرافع للاتحاد والاتصال، (و) يجوز أيضاً ( على المنفصلين ما يجوز على المتصلين من الاتصال الرافع للاثنينية والانفكاكية، وذلك لأن هذه الأربعة متوافقة في الماهية فتكون متشاركة إما في الامتناع عن قبول الانفصال والاتصال أو في جواز قبولهما . والأول باطل قطعا فتعين الثاني، فكل واحد من الأجسام الصغار قابل للاتصال والانفصال ( اللهم إلا لمانع خارج عنه ) وذلك المانع

قوله : (من أن تلك الأجسام المفردة الصغار متوافقة في الماهية النوعية) يعني أن تلك الأجسام المفردة في الجسم المتشابه الأجزاء كالماء كانت متوافقة في الماهية النوعية عند هذا القائل لا في سائر الأجسام المركبة مطلقاً كالمعادن فإن تلك الأجسام المفردة تكون هاهنا متخالفة الماهية عنده أيضا .

قوله : ( اللهم إلا لمانع وتوضيح المقام أن كل جسم منقسم بالقسمة الانفكاكية لا بد أن يحصل فيه قسمان متمايزان في الوجود والاتصال والانفصال الحاصلين بين الأقسام إما لنفس ماهية الجسم أو اللازم له وعلى التقديرين يلزم اتصال المنفصلين وانفصال المتصلين لأن الاشتراك في الماهية يستلزم الاشتراك في اللوازم والأحكام لأن الواحد من تلك الأجسام الصغار قابل للاتصال والانفصال اللهم إلا أن يمنع من قبولهما مانع خارج عنه وذلك المانع لا يكون لازماً لماهية الجسم، وإلا انحصر نوعه في شخصه وإذا لم يكن ،لازماً، فيمكن مفارقة المانع عن
The non-existence of the indivisible part and what under its ruling necessitates that the body is either continuous in itself and thus an individual body, or its composition terminates in individual bodies. So why is it not permissible that the simple body we are considering is composed of individual bodies capable of imaginary division without actual division, so prime matter is not proven by the mentioned proof because it is based on that the continuous body in itself receives external separation, nor is the mathematical body proven either, because those individual bodies do not change in their shapes and magnitudes. And Ibn Sina refuted it — i.e., the statement of this proponent — with what its summary is that every part of them, i.e., of those parts capable of imaginary division, the imaginary division produces in it a duality such that the nature of each of them is the nature of the other, and the nature of the whole — and that is apparent — and the nature of the other part, the external which agrees with it in essence, based on what that proponent holds that those small individual bodies are concordant in specific essence. So then it is permissible for the two connected parts supposed in one part what is permissible for the two separated parts — i.e., the part that divided and the other part — of separation that removes union and connection, and it is also permissible for the two separated parts what is permissible for the two connected parts of connection that removes duality and separation. This is because these four are concordant in essence, so they share either in the impossibility of accepting separation and connection or in the possibility of accepting them both. The first is absolutely false, so the second is determined. So each of the small bodies is capable of connection and separation, unless there is an external impediment. And that impediment — 

His statement: 'that those small individual bodies are concordant in specific essence' means that those individual bodies in a body with homogeneous parts like water are concordant in specific essence according to this proponent, not in all composite bodies absolutely like minerals, for those individual bodies are here diverse in essence according to him as well.

His statement: 'Unless there is an impediment' and clarification of the position: Every body that is divisible by separative division necessarily has in it two distinct parts in existence, and the connection and separation occurring between the parts are either due to the essence of the body itself or its necessary concomitant. On both suppositions, the connection of the separated parts and the separation of the connected parts necessarily follow, because sharing in essence necessitates sharing in concomitants and judgments. Thus one of those small bodies is capable of connection and separation, unless there is an external impediment that prevents them from that. And that impediment is not a necessary concomitant of the essence of the body, otherwise its species would be restricted to its individual. And if it is not necessary, then it is possible for the impediment to separate from
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomistic theory that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajaza'). It begins by stating the objection: if there are no indivisible parts, then body must be either continuous in itself or composed of individual bodies (atoms). Why cannot the simple body be composed of atoms that are only imaginarily divisible? Then Ibn Sina's refutation is presented: if atoms are homogeneous in essence, then imaginary division of one atom produces two parts with the same nature. Since these two parts are identical in essence to other atoms, they must be capable of the same properties as any two separated atoms, i.e., separation and connection. Therefore, each atom must be capable of actual separation, contradicting indivisibility. The only exception is if there is an external impediment, but that impediment cannot be a necessary property of the essence, otherwise the species would be unique to that individual. So atoms cannot be truly indivisible. The commentary then clarifies two points: (1) the homogeneity assumption only applies to bodies with homogeneous parts like water; (2) the external impediment is not a necessary concomitant, so it can be removed, implying that atoms would then be divisible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's argument only works if all atoms in the universe are identical in essence.
Correction: The argument only requires homogeneity within a given body (e.g., all parts of water are identical in essence). The commentator clarifies that for composite bodies like minerals, atoms may differ, but the argument still holds for homogeneous parts.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's argument, what must be true of the two parts produced by imaginary division of an atom?
A) They have different essences from the original atom.
B) They are capable of actual separation and connection.
C) They are indivisible like the original atom.
D) They are only mentally existent.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina shows that if atoms are homogeneous in essence, they must be capable of division, thus atomism collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn Sina says that if two things share the same essence, they share the same potentialities. You share the same human essence as every other believer. What 'external impediment' in your heart prevents you from connecting with the truth that others have embraced? Will you remove it today?
?
The atomist assumed all parts are identical in essence to justify their indivisibility. Do you assume your own flaws are fixed and indivisible, or do you see that Allah has placed in every soul the potential for change and separation from sin? What step will you take to actualize that potential?

Clarifying Shape, Size, and Essence in Atomist Doctrine
Two glosses clarify the meaning of shape/size invariability and agreement in specific essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced positions regarding shape/size invariability and the scope of agreement in specific essence among individual bodies in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Two clarifications reveal disagreement on atomic shapes/sizes and limit essence-agreement to uniform substances.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks two key phrases from the main text. The first reveals that even among those who deny change in atomic shapes and sizes, there was disagreement about whether shapes and sizes vary. The second restricts the claim of agreement in specific essence only to bodies with uniform parts, like water, not to all composites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the two separate 'قوله' (his statement) markers that introduce each gloss.
The passage is short but contains two distinct clarifications.
2
Understand the first gloss on shape/size
The first gloss addresses the phrase 'their shapes and sizes do not change.' It points out that there is disagreement among scholars about whether shapes (e.g., spherical or not) and sizes (small or large) actually vary. This shows that the claim of invariability was not universally accepted.
The commentator is noting a point of contention, not asserting a single position.
3
Analyze the second gloss on specific essence
The second gloss explains the phrase 'those small individual bodies are in agreement in specific essence.' It clarifies that this agreement holds only for bodies with similar parts, like water, and not for all composite bodies like minerals, where individual parts may differ in essence according to the proponent.
This restricts the scope of the claim significantly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لا تتغير أشكالها ومقاديرها أما تغاير أشكالها واختلافها كرية أو غير كرية أو اختلاف مقاديرها صغراً وكبراً ففيه تردد بينهم .
قوله : (من أن تلك الأجسام المفردة الصغار متوافقة في الماهية النوعية) يعني أن تلك الأجسام المفردة في الجسم المتشابه الأجزاء كالماء كانت متوافقة في الماهية النوعية عند هذا القائل لا في سائر الأجسام المركبة مطلقاً كالمعادن فإن تلك الأجسام المفردة تكون هاهنا متخالفة الماهية عنده أيضا .
His statement: (Their shapes and sizes do not change) — As for the variation of their shapes and their being spherical or non-spherical, or the variation of their sizes, small or large, there is disagreement among them. His statement: (That those small individual bodies are in agreement in specific essence) means that those individual bodies in a body similar in its parts, like water, were in agreement in specific essence according to this proponent, not in all composite bodies absolutely, like minerals, for those individual bodies here are of differing essences according to him as well.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal notes (قوله) that explain phrases from the main text. The first note addresses the phrase 'their shapes and sizes do not change' – it points out that there is disagreement among scholars about whether shapes and sizes truly do not vary (e.g., being spherical or not, bigger or smaller). The second note explains the phrase 'those small individual bodies are in agreement in specific essence.' It clarifies that this agreement in specific essence is only claimed for homogeneous bodies like water, not for all composite bodies such as minerals, where the individual parts may differ in essence according to the proponent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the agreement in specific essence applies to all composite bodies uniformly.
Correction: The passage explicitly restricts this agreement to bodies with similar parts (like water) and denies it for other composites like minerals.

The Composition of Body and the Verbal Dispute
Sanusi defines the body as composite and addresses a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Kalam doctrine that the body is composed of hyle and form, and the conditions under which the dispute about the body's definition becomes merely verbal.

Core Idea
The body's essence is the extended substance; matter is the hidden component requiring proof.

Sanusi begins by affirming the composite nature of the body from hyle and form. He clarifies that what we first perceive is the extended substance (the 'connection'), which is the body in initial perception. The matter (hyle) is what needs proof. If one accepts the existence of both matter and form, then the dispute over what the body is becomes purely verbal. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing on details. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the core claims
Find the three main claims: (1) the body is composed of hyle and form, (2) the extended substance is the first thing perceived, (3) matter needs proof. Also see the condition for verbal dispute.
The passage uses technical terms like hayula, sura, ittisal.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
Notice the condition: if one accepts the existence of matter and that one is receptive and the other received, then the dispute about what the body is becomes verbal. Why? Because both sides would agree on the components.
The author suggests this resolution is valid only if the opponent admits two substances.

المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة هذا الاتصال) المقبول ) ولا شك أن الصورة الاتصالية أي الجوهر الممتد في مع الجهات الذي تبين بنفي الجزء اتصاله في نفسه ( أول ما يدرك من جوهرية الجسم ) أي حقيقته بل هو الجسم في بادئ الرأي المعلوم وجوده بالضرورة ) والذي يحتاج إلى الإثبات ( بالدليل ( هو المادة ( المتصفة بذلك الجوهر المتصل، فإذا سلم ثبوتها وأن أحدهما قابل والآخر مقبول (فيصير النزاع) في أن الجسم ماذا نزاعاً لفظياً) لا فائدة فيه. وأنت تعلم أن هذا إنما يصح إذا سلم ذلك القائل أن هناك جوهرين في أن الجسم ذلك القابل فقط أو هو مع الاتصال بهذا المعنى فإنه لا يقول أحد أن الوحدة جزء من الجسم فالوجه ترك هذه المقدمة والاكتفاء بما بعده. قوله ولا شك أن الصورة إلخ) يعني المراد بالاتصال هو الجوهر الممتد ولا شك ثبوته بعد نفي الجزء، وكونه من حقيقة الجسم فإنه الجسم في بادئ الرأي والذي يحتاج إلى الإثبات هو المادة حتى يثبت كونه جزءاً من الجسم فإذا ثبت بقوله : التفريق أمر آخر يكون القابل بالحقيقة حتى لا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية ثبت كون الاتصال بمعنى الجوهر الممتد جزءاً وعدم كونه تمام الحقيقة. قوله : فيصير النزاع إلخ الأولى تركه لأن النزاع في وجود ذلك القابل أو عدمه في الجسم بعد الاتفاق على أن الجسم هو الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة لا أن الجسم ماذا هو . قوله : ( إنما يصح إلخ ) هذا يرد على تقرير الشارح حيث قال : فإذا سلم ثبوتها وأن هناك جوهرين إلخ . وأما على ما قررناه فلا حدوث قلنا : فإذا أثبتنا بقوله : التعريف أمر آخر في الجسم حتى لا يكون التفريق إلخ . كما لا يخفى .
The First Observatory - Seventh Section: The body is composed of hyle and form. This connection (the receptive) and there is no doubt that the form of connection (al-sura al-ittisaliya), i.e. the extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd) in the directions which is proven by negating the part, its connection in itself (is the first thing perceived of the substantiality of the body) i.e. its essence, rather it is the body in the first view, known necessarily to exist; and what needs to be proven by proof is the matter (al-madda) qualified by that extended substance. So if its existence is accepted and that one is receptive and the other is received, then the dispute becomes verbal, with no benefit. And you know that this is only valid if that speaker accepts that there are two substances, regarding whether the body is just the receptive or it is together with connection in this sense, because no one says that unity is a part of the body, so it is better to abandon this premise and suffice with what follows. His saying: 'and there is no doubt that the form etc.' means: the intended meaning of connection is the extended substance, and there is no doubt of its existence after negating the part, and that it is from the reality of the body, for it is the body in the first view; what needs proof is the matter until it is proven that it is a part of the body. So if it is proven by his saying: 'division is another matter, so the receptive is in reality so that division is not complete annihilation,' it is proven that connection in the sense of the extended substance is a part and not the complete reality. His saying: 'then the dispute becomes etc.' it is better to leave it, because the dispute is about the existence or non-existence of that receptive in the body after agreement that the body is the substance receptive to three dimensions, not what the body is. His saying: 'it is only valid etc.' this refutes the commentator's statement where he said: 'if its existence is accepted and that there are two substances etc.' As for what we have established, there is no occurrence; we say: if we prove by his saying: 'definition is another matter in the body so that division is not etc.' as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a definition and initial argument. It begins by stating the doctrine that the body is composed of hyle and form. It then clarifies that the 'connection' (al-ittisal) refers to the extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd), which is the first thing perceived of the body's substantiality; it is known necessarily and is considered the body in initial perception. What needs proof is the matter (al-madda) that qualifies this extended substance. If one accepts the existence of both the receptive (the matter) and the received (the extended substance), then the dispute over what the body is becomes merely verbal—whether it is just the receptive or the receptive together with connection. However, this verbal resolution only holds if the opponent admits the existence of two substances (the extended substance and the matter). The author notes that no one claims that unity is a part of the body, so it is better to drop that premise and rely on what follows. The subsequent commentary elaborates: 'there is no doubt' confirms the extended substance as the intended meaning and its necessity. The proof needed is for the matter as a part. Once that is established, connection as a part is also established. The commentator then critiques the earlier formulation, suggesting that the real dispute is about the existence of the receptive in the body after agreeing that the body is the substance receptive to three dimensions, not about what the body is. The passage ends by showing how the original claim refutes a particular presentation.

The Objection Against Continuity as a Part of Body
Al-Jurjani presents the objection that if continuity is a part of body, the body cannot receive discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that if continuity is a part of the body, the body cannot receive discontinuity, and the contradiction that arises.

Core Idea
A part's removal destroys the whole, so the body cannot receive discontinuity.

This lesson examines a specific objection within the kalam debates on the composition of bodies. The philosopher Al-Jurjani presents a question that targets the claim that continuity is an essential part of the body. He shows that this claim leads to a contradiction: the body must both cease to exist and remain when discontinuity occurs. By following the reasoning closely, you'll see how kalam masters used logical arguments to critique opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two premises: continuity as a part, and body's receptivity to discontinuity.
Notice that the objection is presented as a difficult question.
2
Understand the core contradiction
The objection shows that if continuity is a part, its removal destroys the body's identity, so the body cannot be receptive to it. But if it is receptive, it must remain. This forces a contradiction.
Ask: How does the objector derive the contradiction?
3
Appreciate the argumentative method
Notice that the objector assumes the opponent's premises and shows they lead to a logical impossibility (ijtima' al-mutanafiyayn). This is a standard kalam refutation technique.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this objection means for the theory that body is a continuous substance. Does it refute it? The author may have an answer later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة
جوهراً وراء هذا الجوهر المتصل، لكن المشهور أنه يقول : إن هذا الجوهر المتصل قائم بنفسه وهو حقيقة الجسم ومحل للاتصال الذي هو الوحدة والانفصال الذي هو الكثرة على معنى أنهما عرضان يحلان فيه على التعاقب كما ذهب إليه أفلاطون من أن آخر ما تنحل إليه الأجسام هو هذا الجوهر المتصل الممتد في الجهات كلها فطريق الرد عليه أنه يلزم من ذلك أن يكون التفريق إعداماً للجسم بالكلية وإيجاداً لقسمين آخرين من كتم العدم وهو باطل كما سيأتي تحقيقه ) وهاهنا سؤال يستصعبه بعض و ) ذلك السؤال ( هو أن الاتصال إذا كان جزءا للجسم كما زعمتم ( فبزواله ) الذي الانفصال ( تعدم هوية الجسم لانتفاء الكل بانتفاء جزئه ( فلا يكون الجسم قابلاً له ) أي لزواله أعني الانفصال ( وإذا كان الجسم ( قابلاً لزواله كما ادعيتموه أيضاً، فلا بد أن ( يبقى مع زواله) وإذا بقي معه فليس هو أي الاتصال ( جزءاً للجسم) والحاصل أن كون الجسم قابلاً للانفصال الذي هو زوال الاتصال ينافي كون الاتصال جزءاً له، فقد لزمكم فيما ذهبتم إليه القول باجتماع المتنافيين .
First Observatory - Seventh Purpose: The body is composed of matter and form. A substance behind this continuous substance, but the well-known (view) is that he says: This continuous substance is self-subsistent and is the reality of the body and the locus for continuity which is unity and discontinuity which is multiplicity, meaning that they are two accidents that inhere in it successively, as Plato held that the ultimate resolution of bodies is this continuous substance extended in all directions. The way to refute him is that it follows from that that division would be complete annihilation of the body and bringing into existence two other parts from the concealment of non-existence, and it is false as its verification will come. And here there is a question that some find difficult: That question is: If continuity is a part of the body as you claim, then by its removal—which is discontinuity—the identity of the body is destroyed because the whole ceases with the cessation of its part; so the body is not receptive to it, i.e., to its removal, meaning discontinuity. And if the body is receptive to its removal as you also claim, then it must remain with its removal; and if it remains with it, then continuity is not a part of the body. The upshot is: the body's being receptive to discontinuity, which is the removal of continuity, contradicts continuity's being a part of it. So you have been forced, in what you have gone to, to affirm the conjunction of two opposites.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of a specific objection within a broader discussion on whether the body is composed of matter and form. It first states the view that the continuous substance (al-jawhar al-muttasil) is self-subsistent, is the reality of the body, and is the locus for the accidents of continuity (unity) and discontinuity (multiplicity). It then presents a question that some find difficult: if continuity is a part of the body, then when continuity is removed (i.e., when the body is divided), the body's identity is destroyed because the whole ceases when its part ceases. This would imply that the body cannot be receptive to discontinuity. But if the body is receptive to discontinuity, it must remain when discontinuity occurs. From these two premises—continuity is a part, and the body is receptive to its removal—a contradiction follows: the body cannot both cease and remain upon the removal of continuity. Therefore, the claim that continuity is a part leads to the conjunction of two opposites (ijtima' al-mutanafiyayn).

الاتصال
الانفصال
هوية الجسم
اجتماع المتنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that the body has parts or is composite.
Correction: The objection does not deny composition; it only targets the specific claim that continuity is a part, showing it leads to contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) The body can be divided without losing its identity.
B) The body cannot be receptive to discontinuity because its identity would be destroyed.
C) The body is composed of atoms.
D) Continuity is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses a reductio argument to show that the claim 'continuity is a part of body' is incompatible with the body's receptivity to division, forcing a contradiction.

Resolving the Objection: Two Meanings of 'Continuity'
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection by distinguishing two meanings of 'ittisal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the objection by distinguishing between two meanings of 'continuity' (al-ittisal): one essential and one accidental.

Core Idea
Continuity has two meanings: one never leaves the body, the other can change.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses an objection that seemed powerful by showing it rests on a simple equivocation. The term 'continuity' (al-ittisal) is used in two distinct senses: the substantial form that gives a body its capacity for extension, and the accidental quantity of the extensions themselves. By clarifying these meanings, the objection dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that al-Jurjani begins by identifying the objection as a sophism due to equivocation.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where al-Jurjani distinguishes two meanings of 'continuity'. List them in your own words.
The first meaning is substantial and inseparable; the second is accidental and changeable.
3
Understand the example
Study the example of the body changing shapes while its corporeal form remains. How does this illustrate the distinction?
The example shows that accidental changes in extension do not affect the essential continuity.
4
Consider why this matters
Reflect on why this distinction refutes the objection. Why would confounding the two meanings lead to a false conclusion?
The objection assumed that any discontinuity would destroy the body, but that only applies to the essential meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظن المستصعب أن ذلك السؤال مغالطة وقعت من الاشتراك اللفظي، فإن الاتصال يقال للصورة الجوهرية التي بها للجسم قبول الامتدادات الثالث وهو أمر لا يزول عن الجسم بحال من الأحوال إذ لا يتصور بقاء جسم مع زوال هذه الصورة عنه. ويقال أيضاً لنفس الامتدادات وهو كم وليس جزءاً للجسم لأنه عرض فلا يكون مقوماً للجوهر بل عارضاً له فلا يلزم من زواله زوال الجسم كما إذا شكل الجسم بأشكال مختلفة المقادير مع بقاء صورته الجسمية بعينها
And the one who found it difficult thought that that question is a sophism arising from equivocation (al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī). For 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) is said of the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) by which the body has the reception of the three extensions, and it is something that does not leave the body in any state, since the remaining of a body without this form is inconceivable. And it is also said of the extensions themselves, which is quantity (kamm), and it is not a part of the body because it is an accident; so it does not constitute the substance, but rather is an accident of it, so its removal does not necessitate the removal of the body, just as when the body is shaped into different shapes of various magnitudes while its corporeal form (ṣūratuhu al-jismiyya) remains the same.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the previous objection. It identifies the objection as a sophism (mughālata) arising from equivocation (ishtirāk lafẓī). The term 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) has two distinct meanings: (1) the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) that gives the body the capacity to receive the three dimensions (extension). This meaning is inseparable from the body—a body cannot exist without this form. (2) The extensions themselves (al-imitidādāt), which are accidental quantity (kamm). This quantity is not a part (juz') of the body; it is an accident that inheres in the body and can change without affecting the body's substance. Therefore, the objection fails because it conflates these two meanings. When continuity as the substantial form is meant, the body cannot lose it; when continuity as quantity is meant, its removal (discontinuity) does not destroy the body—just as a body (like wax) can change its shape and size while retaining its substantial form.

Key Terms
الاتصال
الاشتراك اللفظي
الصورة الجوهرية
الامتدادات
الكم
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies any real continuity in the body; it claims continuity is only accidental.
Correction: It actually affirms an unchanging essential continuity (substantial form) while allowing accidental continuity (quantity) to vary.

Checkpoint
What are the two meanings of 'continuity' (al-ittiṣāl) distinguished in the answer?
A) The form and the matter.
B) The substantial form that gives capacity for extension, and the accidental quantity (extensions themselves).
C) The body and the soul.
D) Unity and multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the objection by showing that 'continuity' has two distinct meanings: one essential and inseparable, the other accidental and changeable.

The Argument from Rarefaction and Condensation for Hyle and Its Refutation
Argument from takhallul/takathuf for hyle and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument from rarefaction and condensation for hyle and the refutation based on the bodily form's capacity to receive different magnitudes.

Core Idea
The bodily form itself can receive different magnitudes — no separate hyle is needed.

Philosophers argued that expansion and contraction of bodies requires an underlying substrate not inherently sized, which they called hyle. The Mutakallimun countered that the bodily form (jismiyya) entails dimension in general but not a specific dimension, so it alone can account for changes in size. This passage presents that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for hyle
Read the first part of the passage which presents the proponents' reasoning: real rarefaction and condensation require an entity not intrinsically determined to a magnitude.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument's core premise: if there were no such entity, then size changes would require something joining or separating, which is not observed.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation points out that the bodily form entails dimension in existence and conception, but not a particular dimension, so it can be the receiver of different magnitudes. Thus hyle is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استعانوا في إثبات الهيولى بالتخلخل والتكاثف الحقيقيين، فإنه إذا لم يكن في الجسم أمر غير متقدر بذاته حتى يتصور قبوله للمقادير المختلفة امتنع ازدياد حجمه وانتقاصه من غير انضمار شيء إليه وانفصاله عنه وجوابه أن الصورة الجسمية، وإن كانت مستلزمة في الوجود والتعقل للمقدار إلا أنها لا تستلزم مقداراً مخصوصاً فجاز أن تكون هي قابلة لتلك المقادير المختلفة فلا يثبت وجود أمر آخر
They sought help in proving hyle by means of rarefaction and condensation, both real. For if there were not in the body an entity not intrinsically determined to a particular magnitude, such that it may receive different magnitudes, then an increase in its size and a decrease would be impossible without something joining to it or separating from it. The answer is that the bodily form, although it entails dimension in existence and conception, does not entail a particular dimension, so it is possible that it itself is the receiver of those different magnitudes, and thus the existence of another entity is not established.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an argument used by proponents of hyle (primordial matter) to prove its existence, and then provides the refutation from the Mutakallimun. The argument from rarefaction and condensation claims that a body undergoing expansion or contraction must contain an underlying substrate that is not intrinsically sized, so that it can receive different sizes. The refutation points out that the bodily form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) necessarily entails dimension in existence and conception, but it does not entail a specific dimension. Therefore, the bodily form itself can be the receiver of different magnitudes, eliminating the need for a separate entity called hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة الجسمية
التخلخل والتكاثف
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in magnitude requires a distinct substrate independent of form.
Correction: The bodily form itself can receive different magnitudes because it entails dimension without specifying a particular magnitude.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the refutation draw from the bodily form's relation to magnitude?
A) The bodily form requires hyle to change magnitude.
B) The bodily form does not entail a specific magnitude and thus can receive different magnitudes.
C) The bodily form is identical to hyle.
D) The bodily form is impossible without hyle.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form's indifference to specific magnitude makes it sufficient to account for size changes, undercutting the need for hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does your trust in Allah's creation extend to accepting that change does not threaten your identity? What would it take to let go of the idea that you need a fixed core?

If the bodily form can receive different magnitudes without needing hyle, then Allah has made creation self-sufficient in that respect. Is there an area of your life where you are adding unnecessary 'hyle'—extra beliefs or practices—because you think the original is insufficient? How can you simplify your reliance on what Allah has given?
?
The refutation hinges on the fact that form does not entail a specific magnitude. Are you attached to a specific 'magnitude' in your life—a role, status, or possession—as if it defines you? This lesson shows that the essence (form) can remain while the size changes. Can you find security in your essence as a servant of Allah, even if your circumstances expand or contract?

Argument from Generation and Corruption for Hyle and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument from generation and corruption for hyle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from generation and corruption for hyle and its refutation, including why the argument is considered circular.

Core Idea
Generation and corruption do not prove hyle; the bodily form suffices and the argument is circular.

The argument from generation and corruption (al-kawn wa al-fasād) is one of the classic philosophical proofs for the existence of hyle, the primordial substratum. However, the refutation demonstrates that the argument is flawed on two grounds: the bodily form itself can account for the change, and the argument is circular. This lesson examines both the argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's line of reasoning.
Notice the structure: first the argument is presented, then the refutation is given in two parts.
2
Identify the two prongs of the refutation
The refutation has two independent arguments: (1) the bodily form can serve as the substratum for changing specific forms, and (2) the argument is circular because it presupposes what it aims to prove.
Reflect on how each prong undermines the original argument.
3
Understand the circularity
The argument uses the very phenomenon (generation and corruption) that itself depends on hyle, creating a vicious circle. Ask yourself: how does this make the argument invalid?
Circular arguments prove nothing; they assume the conclusion in the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكون والفساد . أي وربما استعانوا بهما أيضاً إذ لا بد فيهما من أمر يخلع صورة ويلبس أخرى وهو الهيولى وفساده ظاهر لأن المتبدل في الكون والفساد هو الصور النوعية فجاز أن يكون القابل لها خلعاً ولبساً هو الصورة الجسمية على أنا نقول : وجود هذه الأمور التي استعين بها مبني على وجود الهيولى فيلزم الدور.
And generation and corruption. That is, they perhaps also sought help from both, for in them there must be an entity that removes one form and puts on another, which is hyle. But its corruption is apparent, because that which changes in generation and corruption is the specific forms, so it is possible that the receiver for them in removal and putting on is the bodily form. Except that we say: the existence of these things which are used as aids is built upon the existence of hyle, so circularity follows.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents another argument for hyle based on generation and corruption (substantial change). The argument states that when one thing becomes another, there must be a persistent substrate (hyle) that loses one form and acquires another. The refutation points out that what changes are the specific forms (al-ṣuwar al-nawʿiyya), and the bodily form could be the substratum that receives the forms. Moreover, the argument is circular because the very phenomena used to prove hyle (generation and corruption) themselves presuppose hyle, leading to a vicious circle. Thus, the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
الكون والفساد
الصور النوعية
الهيولى
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substantial change necessarily requires a substratum distinct from the bodily form (i.e., hyle).
Correction: The bodily form can serve as the substratum for the change of specific forms, so hyle is not proven by generation and corruption.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity mentioned in the refutation?
A) The argument uses hyle to prove hyle.
B) The existence of generation and corruption depends on hyle, which is what is being proved.
C) The bodily form depends on hyle.
D) The specific forms depend on hyle.

Key Takeaway
The argument from generation and corruption for hyle is refuted because the bodily form can serve as the substratum and the argument is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever argue for something based on an assumption that itself relies on what you are trying to prove? Can you identify a belief you hold that might be circular?
?
The refutation suggests that the bodily form is sufficient to explain change. How content are you with simple explanations for complex phenomena, or do you feel the need for an underlying 'something'?
?
When you read this passage, did you instinctively side with the proponents of hyle because their argument felt intuitive? What does this reveal about your reliance on intuitive assumptions in matters of faith and reason?

The Mutakallimun's Main Argument Against Hyle: The Three Branches on Spatiality
Mutakallimun's argument against hyle via three spatial branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mutakallimun's decisive argument that negates the existence of hyle by analyzing its possible relations to space and showing that each leads to absurdity or contradicts its definition.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot be a substratum because any relation to space makes it either a body, an attribute, or a non-entity.

The Mutakallimun (rational theologians) attacked the Aristotelian concept of hyle (prime matter) by focusing on its relation to space. They argued that if hyle exists, it must be either in space or not. Both options lead to contradictions that prove hyle cannot serve as the substratum of forms. This lesson walks through the three branches of that argument and examines a possible response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomous setup
The argument begins by dividing the supposition of hyle's existence into two possibilities: having spatiality or not. This sets the stage for the three branches.
2
Examine the first branch: independent spatiality
If hyle occupies space independently, it would be a body, causing problems: the bodily form would be like it, leading to infinite regress or dependence issues.
This branch is further supported by multiple sub-arguments.
3
Examine the second branch: dependent spatiality
If hyle occupies space dependently via the bodily form, it becomes an attribute of the form, not a substratum.
4
Examine the third branch: no spatiality
If hyle has no spatiality, it is a pure intelligible incapable of being a locus for a spatial form.
5
Consider the response
A possible answer reinterprets dependent spatiality as the bodily form inhering in hyle, but this does not ultimately save hyle.
The response is part of the dialectic, but the Mutakallimun maintain their negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد عند المتكلمين في نفي الهيولى أنها على تقدير وجودها إما أن يكون لها حصول الحيز أو لا يكون. فإن كان لها حصول فيه فإما أن يكون ذلك الحصول على سبيل الاستقلال فجسم أي فالهيولى جسم لأن المتحيز بالذات لا بد أن يكون جوهراً ممتداً في الجهات ولا معنى للجسم إلا ذلك. وأيضاً فالصورة الجسمية حينئذ مثل لها، فكيف تحل فيها؟ وأيضاً إن احتاجت الهيولى إلى محل لزم التسلسل، وإلا كانت الجسمية مستغنية عن المحل لأنها مثلها. أو لا يكون ذلك الحصول على سبيل الاستقلال بل على سبيل التبعية للصورة الجسمية فالهيولى حينئذ صفة حالة في الجسمية تابعة لها في التحيز لا جوهر، هو محل لها كما هو مطلوبكم. وإلا أي وإن لم يكن لها حصول في الحيز لا استقلالاً ولا تبعاً فلا تختص الجسمية بها اختصاصاً ناعتاً لها لأنه أي لأن ما لا تحيز له أصلاً أمر معقول محض لا تعلق ولا اختصاص له بحيز قطعاً فكيف يتصور حلول الجسمية المتحيزة بالذات فيه؟ وقد يجاب بأنا لا نسلم أنها لو كانت متحيزة بالتبعية لكانت صفة للجسمية فإن تحيز الشيء بالتبعية قد يكون باعتبار حلوله في الغير، كما في الأعراض الحالة في الأجسام، وقد يكون باعتبار حلول الغير فيه، فليس يلزم من تحيز الهيولى لا بالاستقلال أن يكون تحيزها على سبيل حلولها في الجسمية بل يجوز أن يكون تحيزها بشرط حلول الجسمية فيها فتكون موصوفة بها لا صفة لها.
And the relied-upon [argument] of the Mutakallimun in negating hyle is that, on the supposition of its existence, either it has possession of space or not. If it has possession in it, then either that possession is independent, so it is a body — that is, hyle is a body, because that which is intrinsically spatial must be a substance extended in directions, and the meaning of body is nothing but that. Also, the bodily form would then be its like; how then could it inhere in it? Also, if hyle needs a locus, then there would be an infinite regress; otherwise, the bodily form would be independent of a locus because it is like it. Or that possession is not independent but instead dependent on the bodily form; then hyle is at that time an attribute inhering in the bodily form, following it in spatiality, not a substance that is a locus for it as you claim. Otherwise, that is, if it has no possession in space, neither independently nor dependently, then the bodily form does not have a characterizing connection with it, because that which has no spatiality at all is a pure intelligible, having absolutely no relation or connection to space; so how can the inherence of the bodily form, which is intrinsically spatial, be conceived in it? And it may be answered: we do not accept that if it were spatially dependent then it would be an attribute of the bodily form, for the spatiality of a thing dependently may be by virtue of its inherence in another, as in accidents inhering in bodies, or by virtue of the inherence of another in it; so it does not follow from hyle's not having independent spatiality that its spatiality is by way of its inherence in the bodily form; rather, it is possible that its spatiality is conditioned by the inherence of the bodily form in it, so that it is described by it, not an attribute of it.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the decisive argument of the Mutakallimun against hyle, structured as a dichotomy regarding hyle's relation to space (al-ḥayyiz). The argument proceeds in three branches: (1) If hyle has independent spatial occupation, then it is a body — which contradicts its role as a substratum, since the bodily form would then be similar to it and could not inhere in it, and an infinite regress would ensue. (2) If hyle has dependent spatial occupation (i.e., by virtue of the bodily form), then it is merely an attribute of the bodily form, not a separate substance. (3) If hyle has no spatial occupation at all, then it is a pure intelligible abstraction incapable of being a locus for the bodily form, which is intrinsically extended. The passage then presents a possible response: dependent spatiality need not mean the hyle inheres in the bodily form; it could mean the bodily form inheres in hyle, making hyle the substratum described by spatiality, not an attribute. However, this response is not the final word; the argument overall favors the negation of hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الحيز
الاستقلال
التبعية
الصورة الجسمية
المتحيز بالذات
أمر معقول محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the response proves hyle can exist if it has dependent spatiality interpreted as the bodily form inhering in it.
Correction: The response is a potential objection, but the Mutakallimun would reject it because the bodily form would still need a substratum, and hyle as a pure intelligible cannot provide extension.

Checkpoint
According to the Mutakallimun, what follows if hyle has independent spatial occupation?
A) Hyle would be an accident.
B) Hyle would be a body, leading to problems like similarity with the bodily form and infinite regress.
C) Hyle would be a pure intelligible.
D) Hyle would be dependent on the bodily form.

Key Takeaway
The Mutakallimun's argument systematically shows that hyle cannot fulfill its role as a substratum regardless of its relation to space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After following this precise logical dismantling of hyle, do you see your own intellect as a gift that demands rigorous use in matters of faith? Can you point to a time when you used reason to defend a belief, and what did that cost you?
?
Consider that hyle, in the third branch, becomes a 'pure intelligible' with no connection to the physical. Is there any belief you hold about the unseen that, upon honest examination, becomes just an abstract concept with no real grounding? Are you willing to test it as the Mutakallimun tested hyle?
?
This argument forces a choice: either hyle is a body, an attribute, or nothing at all. In your spiritual life, what 'hyle'—some assumption or comfort—are you clinging to that, when examined, leads only to contradictions? Will you let it go for the sake of a sounder foundation?
