The Argument from Conceptual Understanding and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument that body is not composed of hyle based on conceptual understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that since we conceive body without conceiving hyle, body is not composed of hyle, and the response that this only works if we have exhaustive knowledge of body's essence.

Core Idea
Conceiving something without its parts does not prove the parts are unreal.

This passage presents an objection against the existence of hyle based on how we conceptualize body. The objectors argue that if body were composed of hyle and form, then thinking of body would require thinking of both. But we think of body without thinking of hyle. The defenders respond by distinguishing between ordinary conception and full essential knowledge. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then premise, false consequent, conclusion that premise is false.
The argument uses conditional reasoning.
2
Focus on the key terms
Pay attention to 'taʿaqqul' (conceptualization), 'ḥaqīqa' (reality), and 'bi-l-kunh' (in essence). These are crucial for the response.
The response denies that we grasp the body's reality fully.
3
Understand the response's nuance
The response does not deny that we conceptualize body; it denies that our conceptualization captures the entire essence. Thus hyle could be part of the reality without being part of our ordinary concept.
This is a common move in kalām: distinguishing between ordinary knowledge and essential knowledge.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument shows that kalām debates often hinge on the extent of human knowledge. The response protects the possibility of hyle by limiting the scope of our conceptual grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في نفي الهيولى وإبطال تركب الجسم منها لو كان الجسم مركباً من جزأين كما ذكرتم لزم من تعقله تعقلهما ولم يحتج في ثبوت شيء منهما له إلى برهان واللازم باطل فإنا نعقل الجسم ولا نعقل الهيولى ونحتاج في إثباتها إلى البرهان والجواب منع تعقل حقيقته يعني أن ما ذكرتم إنما يلزم إذا كان حقيقة الجسم معقولة بالكنه وهو ممنوع.
And it may be said in negating hyle and invalidating the composition of body from it: if the body were composed of two parts, as you have mentioned, then from conceptualizing it would follow conceptualization of them, and no proof would be needed for establishing something of them for it. But the consequent is false, for we conceptualize the body and do not conceptualize hyle, and we need a proof for establishing it. The answer: the denial of conceptualizing its reality, meaning that what you have mentioned would follow only if the reality of the body were conceptualized in its essence, which is denied.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument against hyle based on conceptualization (taʿaqqul). The argument states that if the body were composed of two parts (hyle and form), then understanding the body would require understanding those parts. But we understand the body without understanding hyle, and we need proof to establish hyle. Therefore, the premise is false. The response (al-jawāb) is that this would follow only if the reality (ḥaqīqa) of the body were fully grasped in its essence (bi-l-kunh). Since we do not have exhaustive knowledge of the body's essence, it is possible that hyle is part of its reality without being included in our ordinary conception. Thus, the argument does not disprove hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
تعقل
حقيقة
برهان
كنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can think of body without thinking of hyle, then hyle cannot be a real part of body.
Correction: The response shows that this reasoning assumes our concept fully captures the essence, which it does not. Hyle may be part of the reality without being included in our ordinary thought.

Checkpoint
How do the defenders of hyle respond to the argument from conceptualization?
A) They deny that we conceptualize the body.
B) They argue that we do conceptualize hyle implicitly.
C) They say the argument only works if we fully grasp the body's reality in its essence, which we do not.
D) They admit the argument is valid.

Key Takeaway
The argument from conceptualization fails because our ordinary conception of body does not exhaust its essential reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself rejecting a belief because you cannot conceive of it, pause. Is your inability to conceive proof of falsehood? Name one Islamic belief you struggle to conceptualize. Will you commit to seeking proof rather than dismissing it based on your mental limits?
?
The argument assumes our understanding is exhaustive. Where in your life have you mistaken your limited perception for reality? How can you cultivate humility about your own conceptual framework?
?
If you cannot grasp the essence (kunh) of even a physical body, how much less can you grasp the nature of Allah? Does this challenge your faith or deepen it? What step will you take to trust in proof over personal conception?

The Response: Denying the Unity of Body-Form
The author responds to Ibn Sina by denying the unity of bodily nature and clarifying the role of divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part response to Ibn Sina's argument: the denial that the bodily nature is a single specific nature, and the clarification that divisibility is only a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause.

Core Idea
The bodily nature is ambiguous like quantity, not a fixed single essence.

In this passage, the author counters Ibn Sina's argument that all bodies need matter because of their divisible nature. He launches a two-pronged response: first, he denies that the bodily nature is a single specific nature; second, he clarifies that divisibility is merely a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause. This subtle distinction preserves the possibility that matter is required by the essence of body itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main moves
Read the response and identify the two distinct moves: the denial of unity and the conditional analysis of divisibility. Notice how they interconnect.
The passage shifts from a decisive denial to a hypothetical concession.
2
Understand the analogy with quantity
Focus on the claim that the bodily nature is 'ambiguous like quantity' and cannot exist without constitutive differentiate. Reflect on why this undercuts Ibn Sina's premise.
This is a critical insight: bodily nature is not a ready-made essence.
3
Grasp the logical point about divisibility
The author argues that even if bodily connection were a single nature, divisibility is only a middle term in our affirmation of the need for matter, not a real cause. Otherwise, matter would be posterior to division, which is impossible.
This subtle point preserves the essential need for matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع اتحاد الاتصال الجسمي ) ، أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة الجسمية طبيعة واحدة نوعية ) وذلك مما لا سبيل إلى إثباته ) فإن ما ذكرتموه من اختلافها بالأمور الخارجية عنها مسلم لكن انحصار اختلافها فيه ممنوع فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مطلقاً أمر مبهم كالمقدار، فلا يتصور وجودها إلا بأن يتنوع بفصول مقومة لها، أو بعد تنوعها ينضم إليها أمور خارجة عنها فلم قلتم : إنها ليست كذلك ؟ ( وإن سلم ( أن الاتصال الجسمي حقيقة واحدة نوعية ( فقد ) يجوز و يكون الاحتياج إلى المادة مقتضى ذاته . قلت : قبول الانفصال واسطة في التصديق بالاحتياج وليس بواسطة في الثبوت، وإلا لكان ثبوت الهيولى للأجسام متأخراً عن قبول الانفصال فتدبر فإنه دقيق .
The answer: denial of the unity of bodily connection, i.e., we do not accept that the bodily nature is a single specific nature. And that is something that cannot be proven. For what you mentioned about its differences being due to external things is granted, but the restriction of its differences to that is denied. For the bodily nature absolutely is an ambiguous thing like quantity; its existence cannot be conceived except by being diversified by constitutive differentiate, or after its diversification external things are joined to it. So why do you say it is not like that? And if it is granted that bodily connection is a single specific nature, then it may be that the need for matter is a requirement of its essence. I say: acceptance of division is a middle term in the affirmation of the need, not a middle term in the reality; otherwise the existence of matter for bodies would be posterior to the acceptance of division, so reflect, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the response to Ibn Sina's argument. The author denies that the bodily nature is a single specific nature, arguing that it is ambiguous like quantity and requires intrinsic differentiate to exist. He also argues that even if it were a specific nature, its need for matter could be essential rather than derived from its nature. Finally, he clarifies that the original argument's basis, division, is only a means of knowing the need for matter, not the cause of that need itself. Otherwise, matter would be posterior to division, which is impossible.

Key Terms
اتصال جسمي (bodily connection)
طبيعة نوعية (specific nature)
مبهم (ambiguous)
فصول مقومة (constitutive differentiate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response completely denies that bodies need matter (hylomorphism).
Correction: The response does not deny the need for matter; it only denies Ibn Sina's specific proof from the unity of bodily nature. The author leaves open that matter may be required essentially.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does the author compare the bodily nature to?
A) A single specific nature
B) Quantity
C) An external accident
D) Matter itself

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the proof for matter by denying the unity of bodily nature and showing that divisibility is only epistemic, not the real cause of the need for matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that the need for matter could be an essential requirement of the bodily nature itself, not derived from divisibility. Think about your own spiritual needs: Are they merely a response to external circumstances, or are they essential to your very nature as a created being? How does this shift your understanding of your constant dependence on Allah?
?
The author points out that divisibility is only a means to affirm the need, not the real cause. In your life, when you feel distant from Allah, do you often look for a 'divisibility'—a specific reason or event—rather than recognizing that the need for Him is inherent in your nature? What would change if you internalized that your need for Allah is not contingent on any particular circumstance?

Body-ness as a Specific Nature
Defense of body-ness as a uniform specific nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that body-ness qua body-ness is a single specific nature that uniformly requires matter.

Core Idea
Body-ness is a uniform nature that demands matter in all bodies.

The commentator now defends Ibn Sina's argument against an objection that denies body-ness is a specific nature. This passage establishes that body-ness, considered purely as substantial extension, is a single simple nature with no internal differences. From this, it follows that if any body needs matter, all bodies must need it — because the nature is the same. Let's see how this unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله: أي لا نسلم' to 'فقد بان أن الأجسام مؤلفة من مادة وصورة'. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the definition of body-ness.
The commentator is refuting the denial that body-ness is a specific nature.
2
Identify the nature claim
Notice that body-ness is defined as 'substantial extension' and is called a 'single simple nature'. The commentator argues that differences (like hot/cold) are external to body-ness.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since body-ness is uniform, if it requires matter in one body, it must require it in all. This shows that all bodies are composed of matter and form.
This refutes the objection that some bodies might not need matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة إلخ ) هذا المنع مدفوع لأن المقصود أن الجسمية من حيث جسمية أي امتداد جوهري طبيعة نوعية لكونها موجودة في الخارج من غير اعتبار أمر آخر متحد معها . بل إنما يعتبر من حيث جسميته إلى المادة في العنصريات كانت كذلك في الكل في الشفاء، أما الصورة الجسمية من حيث هي جسمية فهي طبيعة واحدة بسيطة محصلة لا اختلاف فيها ولا يخالف مجرد صورة جسمية لمجرد صورة جسمية بفصل داخل في الجسمية. وما يلحقها إنما يلحقها على أنها شيء خارج عن طبيعتها، فلا يجوز إذا أن تكون جسمية محتاجة إلى مادة وجسمية غير محتاجة إلى مادة واللواحق الخارجية لا بعينها محتاجة إلى المادة بوجه من الوجوه لأن الحاجة إلى المادة إنما تكون للجمسية ولكل ذي مادة وصورة لأجل ذاته، وللجمسية من حيث هي جسمية لاحقة فقد بان أن الأجسام مؤلفة من مادة وصورة انتهى . ولا يخفى أنه كما يندفع بهذا البيان منع كونها طبيعة نوعية لا احتياج فيه إلى إثبات عدم الواسطة بين الاحيتاج في الغنى الذاتيين فإنه استدلال بأن مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يتخلف عنه، فلا يختلف الاحتياج عنها في جسم من الأجسام سواء كان بينهما واسطة أو لا، فتدبر حق التدبر يظهر لك الحق الصريح . قوله : ( فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مضناً إلخ) هذا مكابرة فإنه بعد نفي وجود الجزء وما في حكمه ثبت وجود جوهر لا مفصل فيه والمبهم لا وجود له في الخارج، نعم لمفهوم المأخوذ منه
His saying: 'We do not accept that the nature, etc.' – this denial is refuted, because what is intended is that body-ness qua body-ness, i.e., substantial extension, is a specific nature because it exists externally without considering any other thing united with it. Rather, it is considered qua its body-ness in relation to matter in elemental bodies, and it was like that in all (bodies) in the Shifa. As for the bodily form qua bodily form, it is a single simple acquired nature with no difference in it, and a mere bodily form does not differ from a mere bodily form by a differentiate internal to body-ness. What attaches to it attaches to it as something external to its nature. Therefore, it is not permissible that a body-form should need matter and another body-form not need matter, and external accidents are not themselves in need of matter in any way, because the need for matter is for body-ness and for every possessor of matter and form for its own sake, and for body-ness qua body-ness it is a consequent. Thus it has become clear that bodies are composed of matter and form. End. And it is not hidden that as this explanation refutes the denial of its being a specific nature, there is no need to prove the absence of a middle term between need and self-sufficiency, for it is a proof that the requirement of a specific nature does not fail to apply, so need does not differ concerning it in any body, whether there is a middle term or not. So reflect deeply, and the plain truth will appear to you. His saying: 'For the bodily nature is ambiguous, etc.' – this is a stubborn denial, because after denying the existence of a part and what is in its judgment, the existence of a substance with no division in it is established, and the ambiguous has no existence externally. Yes, for the concept taken from it
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's refutation of the response's denial. He argues that body-ness as such is a specific nature because it exists in the external world without needing intrinsic differentiate to constitute it. The differences that attach to it (like hot/cold) are external. Therefore, if body-ness needs matter in some bodies, it must need it in all, because its nature is uniform. The commentator also criticizes the opponent's claim that the bodily nature is ambiguous, stating that after rejecting atomism (the part), the existence of a substance with no internal division is established, and the ambiguous has no external existence. The passage concludes with an exhortation to reflect deeply.

Key Terms
جسمية من حيث جسمية

طبيعة واحدة بسيطة

A) Because it requires intrinsic differentiate to exist.
B) Because it exists externally without any other thing united with it.
C) Because it is ambiguous like quantity.
D) Because it is purely mental.

Key Takeaway
Body-ness as a specific nature is uniform, so its need for matter is universal in all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If body-ness demands matter uniformly, what uniform spiritual discipline does your soul demand regardless of your mood or circumstances? Have you been inconsistent because you treated it as optional?
?
The commentator urges deep reflection to see the plain truth. What truth about your relationship with Allah have you been avoiding because it would require a change in your daily habits? Will you set aside 10 minutes tonight to reflect on that truth?
?
External accidents do not change the essence of body-ness. What external distractions have you allowed to change the essence of your devotion (ikhlas)? How can you strip them away and return to the core?

Refuting the Ambiguity of Bodily Nature
Refutation of the claim that bodily nature is ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the claim that the bodily nature is ambiguous, by establishing the existence of an indivisible substance.

Core Idea
The bodily nature is not ambiguous; an indivisible substance exists externally.

The opponent had claimed that the bodily nature is ambiguous (mubham), meaning it does not refer to a determinate reality. The commentator responds sharply, calling this a stubborn denial. He argues that after rejecting atomism (the part), the existence of a substance with no internal division is established. Such a substance is not ambiguous — it is a definite reality. The ambiguous concept, he adds, is merely a mental abstraction. Let's see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله : ( فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مضناً إلخ)' to the end. Then read the translation. Identify the opponent's claim and the commentator's response.
The word 'مضناً' is likely an OCR error for 'مطلقاً' (absolutely).
2
Understand the key move
The commentator says: after denying the existence of a part (i.e., atom), we prove the existence of a substance with no division. This substance is not ambiguous; the ambiguous is only a concept in the mind.
3
Grasp the implication
If the bodily nature were ambiguous, it could not exist externally. But we have established an external substance, so the nature is determinate. This reinforces the earlier conclusion that body-ness is a specific nature.
This connects back to the previous lesson's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة إلخ ) هذا المنع مدفوع لأن المقصود أن الجسمية من حيث جسمية أي امتداد جوهري طبيعة نوعية لكونها موجودة في الخارج من غير اعتبار أمر آخر متحد معها . بل إنما يعتبر من حيث جسميته إلى المادة في العنصريات كانت كذلك في الكل في الشفاء، أما الصورة الجسمية من حيث هي جسمية فهي طبيعة واحدة بسيطة محصلة لا اختلاف فيها ولا يخالف مجرد صورة جسمية لمجرد صورة جسمية بفصل داخل في الجسمية. وما يلحقها إنما يلحقها على أنها شيء خارج عن طبيعتها، فلا يجوز إذا أن تكون جسمية محتاجة إلى مادة وجسمية غير محتاجة إلى مادة واللواحق الخارجية لا بعينها محتاجة إلى المادة بوجه من الوجوه لأن الحاجة إلى المادة إنما تكون للجمسية ولكل ذي مادة وصورة لأجل ذاته، وللجمسية من حيث هي جسمية لاحقة فقد بان أن الأجسام مؤلفة من مادة وصورة انتهى . ولا يخفى أنه كما يندفع بهذا البيان منع كونها طبيعة نوعية لا احتياج فيه إلى إثبات عدم الواسطة بين الاحيتاج في الغنى الذاتيين فإنه استدلال بأن مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يتخلف عنه، فلا يختلف الاحتياج عنها في جسم من الأجسام سواء كان بينهما واسطة أو لا، فتدبر حق التدبر يظهر لك الحق الصريح . قوله : ( فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مضناً إلخ) هذا مكابرة فإنه بعد نفي وجود الجزء وما في حكمه ثبت وجود جوهر لا مفصل فيه والمبهم لا وجود له في الخارج، نعم لمفهوم المأخوذ منه
His saying: 'We do not accept that the nature, etc.' – this denial is refuted, because what is intended is that body-ness qua body-ness, i.e., substantial extension, is a specific nature because it exists externally without considering any other thing united with it. Rather, it is considered qua its body-ness in relation to matter in elemental bodies, and it was like that in all (bodies) in the Shifa. As for the bodily form qua bodily form, it is a single simple acquired nature with no difference in it, and a mere bodily form does not differ from a mere bodily form by a differentiate internal to body-ness. What attaches to it attaches to it as something external to its nature. Therefore, it is not permissible that a body-form should need matter and another body-form not need matter, and external accidents are not themselves in need of matter in any way, because the need for matter is for body-ness and for every possessor of matter and form for its own sake, and for body-ness qua body-ness it is a consequent. Thus it has become clear that bodies are composed of matter and form. End. And it is not hidden that as this explanation refutes the denial of its being a specific nature, there is no need to prove the absence of a middle term between need and self-sufficiency, for it is a proof that the requirement of a specific nature does not fail to apply, so need does not differ concerning it in any body, whether there is a middle term or not. So reflect deeply, and the plain truth will appear to you. His saying: 'For the bodily nature is ambiguous, etc.' – this is a stubborn denial, because after denying the existence of a part and what is in its judgment, the existence of a substance with no division in it is established, and the ambiguous has no existence externally. Proof of Hyle from Determinate Place
Iji presents the first proof for hyle from the necessity of a determinate place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hyle is necessary as a substratum for a body to occupy a determinate place.

Core Idea
Hyle is needed because without it, a body would have no reason to be in one place rather than another.

Before we can accept the existence of prime matter (hyle), we need a rational proof. Iji begins with a simple observation: every body occupies a place. But what makes it occupy one specific place over another? He argues that the bodily form alone cannot provide that specification, so hyle must exist as a neutral substratum that receives specification from something else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Iji sets up the necessity of a place for every body.
The passage is the first part of the source chunk, ending with 'لا معيناً.'
2
Understand the necessity of a determinate place
Iji states that a body cannot be in two places at once, and without a specifier it would have no reason to be in one specific place. Grasp this foundational idea.
3
Analyze why bodily form alone is insufficient
The bodily form only demands an absolute place, not a specific one. So it cannot serve as the specifier. This forces the need for hyle as a neutral substrate.
Think: if the form determined a specific place, it would have to be a particular form, but bodily form is generic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم
جسم، وكل جسم لا بد له من حيز ولا يمكن أن يكون جسم واحد في زمان واحد في مكانين أو أكثر، والأخير باطل لعدم المخصص بالنسبة إلى ذلك البعض، لأن الهيولى على ذلك التقدير نسبتها إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية وكذا نسبة الصورة الجسمية، فإنها تقتضي حيزاً مطلقاً لا معيناً. فإن قيل : لعل صورة نوعية تحل في الهيولى مع حلول الصورة الجسمية فيها فهي تخصصها بحيز معين. وأيضا ينتقض ما ذكرتم بالجزء المعين من الأرض ومن سائر العناصر الكلية واختصاصه بحيزه المعين بلا مخصص يقتضيه، فإن نسبة أجزاء العنصر الكلي إلى أجزاء حيزه على السواء مع أن كل واحد من أجزائه حاصل في حيز معين. قلنا : الصورة النوعية وإن عينت موضعاً كلياً لكن نسبتها إلى جميع أجزاء حيز الكل واحدة، فالكلام في تخصيصه بحيزه المعين من أجزاء حيز الكل. فإن الهيولى المجسمة مع تلك الصورة النوعية إما أن تحصل في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، أو في بعضها أو لا تحصل في شيء منها، والكل باطل.
First Observatory - Eighth Objective: Proving Hyle in the Body.
Body, and every body must have a place and it is impossible for one body to be in two places or more at one time, and the latter is false due to the absence of a specifier for that particular part, because hyle on that estimation has equal relation to all places, and likewise the relation of the bodily form, for it requires an absolute place not a specific one. If it is said: Perhaps a specific form indwells in the hyle along with the indwelling of the bodily form in it, so it specifies it to a specific place. Also what you mentioned is refuted by the specific part of earth and of all the universal elements, and its specification to its specific place without a specifier that necessitates it, for the relation of the parts of the universal element to the parts of its place is equal while each one of its parts is obtained in a specific place. We say: The specific form, although it determines a universal location, but its relation to all parts of the place of the whole is one, so the discussion is about its specification to its specific place from the parts of the place of the whole. For the hyle with that specific form either occurs in each one of those parts, or in some of them, or occurs in none of them, and all are false.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of hyle (prime matter) in physical bodies. It begins by stating that every body must occupy a place, and cannot be in two places at once; if hyle were without form, it would have equal relation to all places, so a specifier is needed. The objection raises that a specific form could provide that specification. The reply argues that the specific form only determines a universal location, not a particular part, so the problem of specification remains. The hyle would have to occupy all, some, or none of the parts—all impossible—thus the objection fails.

صورة جسمية
حيز
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the bodily form alone can determine a specific place.
Correction: Iji shows the bodily form only requires an absolute place, not a specific one; it cannot be the specifier.

Checkpoint
What does Iji claim about the bodily form's relation to place?
A) It determines a specific place.
B) It requires an absolute place, not a specific one.
C) It has no relation to place.
D) It requires multiple places.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form alone cannot specify a determinate place, so a neutral substratum (hyle) is required to receive specification from a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Iji proves that without a specifier, hyle relates equally to all places, having no reason to be in one rather than another. Reflect on your own life: What 'specifiers' have determined the path you are on—your career, your city, your relationships? Have you ever considered that without God's specification, you would have no meaningful direction? How does this realization compel you to seek His guidance in your next decision?
?
The bodily form only demands an absolute place, not a specific one. This means that your physical existence alone cannot give you a unique purpose or location. What are the specific influences (family, society, your own choices) that have given your life a particular shape? Are you conscious that these are ultimately secondary to the divine Specifier? How can you realign your life to be more receptive to God's specification?
?
In the argument, the need for a specifier arises because the body cannot be in two places at once. This points to a fundamental limitation of material existence. How does this limitation reflect the human condition—you cannot be in two spiritual states simultaneously? What does this teach you about the need for a single, focused commitment to God, rather than a divided heart?

Reply to the Specific Form Objection
Iji presents and refutes the specific form objection to his proof for hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji refutes the objection that a specific form could serve as the specifier of place.

Core Idea
A specific form only determines a universal location, not a particular part, so it cannot explain why this part of matter is here.

The objection claims that a specific form (ṣūrat al-nawʿiyya) entering the hyle together with the bodily form could specify it to a particular place. Iji responds by showing that even with a specific form, the hyle would still be indifferent to the sub-parts of that universal place. The problem of particularization remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage for the objection and reply, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, counterexample, response.
This passage is the second half of the source chunk.
2
Understand the objection
The objector suggests a specific form (e.g., the form of earth) dwelling in hyle could specify its place. They also give an example of a part of earth occupying a specific place naturally.
3
Analyze Iji's response
Iji concedes the specific form determines a universal location (e.g., the whole region of earth), but it does not determine which particular part of that region. He then presents three possibilities for the hyle's relation to the sub-parts and shows each is false.
The three possibilities: hyle in all sub-parts, some, or none.
4
Appreciate the argument's force
Since all three options are impossible, the objection fails. The need for a specifier beyond the specific form remains, reinforcing the proof for hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Objection: Parts of Universal Elements and the Reply
Iji presents and responds to an objection about specification of parts of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun defend the need for a specifier against objections regarding the parts of universal elements.

Core Idea
Hypothetical parts do not need real specifiers, but actual specification requires a cause.

The second objection challenges the need for a specifier, suggesting that forms or states can determine place, and that isolated hyle might itself be universal. The reply carefully distinguishes between hypothetical parts and real ones, and explains how a chain of previous forms accounts for the specification of actual parts of earth. This passage sharpens the argument for a specifier by addressing subtle metaphysical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that a form or state could specify parts of place for hyle, and that isolated hyle might already be universal, so no extra specifier is needed.
Notice that the objection tries to remove the need for a specifier altogether.
2
Understand the initial reply
The reply first argues that the parts of a universal element are merely hypothetical (مفروضة), not externally existing, so they don't require places.
This is a key distinction between conceptual and real existence.
3
Follow the analysis of earth's part
The reply then explains that a specific part of earth gets its place because its matter had a previous form that determined it to that or another place, from which it moved rectilinearly.
The chain of previous forms goes back infinitely, showing that specification is not arbitrary.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The specifier is the previous position obtained from a prior form, and this chain extends infinitely, implying each specification has a cause.
This supports the overall argument for a first specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : جاز أن يقارن الهيولى صورة أخرى أو حالة من الأحوال تعين لها بعض أجزاء المكان الكلي، وأيضاً قد تكون الهيولى المجردة هيولى عنصر كلي، فلا حاجة في التخصيص إلى غير الصورة النوعية. فإن قلت : ننقل الكلام إلى اختصاص أجزاء ذلك العنصر بأمكنتها الجزئية، قلنا : تلك الأجزاء مفروضة فيه لا موجودة في الخارج فلا تقتضي مكاناً. وأيضاً جاز أن يفرض هناك حالة مخصصة للأجزاء بوضع معين. والجزء من الأرض إنما اختص بحيزه المعين الذي هو فيه لكون مادته قبل تلك الصورة الأرضية كانت لها صورة أخرى مخصصة لذلك الجزء بذلك الحيز، أو مخصصة له بحيز آخر انتقل ذلك الجزء منه بالاستقامة إلى ذلك الحيز. والحاصل أن مخصص ذلك الجزء من الأرض بحيزه المعين هو الوضع السابق الحاصل لمادته بسبب صورة سابقة إما في ذلك الحيز أو في حيز آخر انتقل ذلك الجزء بعد حصول صورته الأرضية منه إلى حيزه على أقرب الطرق، وتلك الصورة السابقة مسبوقة بصورة ثالثة، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية.
And it may be said: It is possible that another form or a state from among the states accompanies the hyle and determines for it some parts of the universal place, and also the isolated hyle may be the hyle of a universal element, so there is no need in specification for other than the specific form. For if you say: we transfer the discussion to the specification of the parts of that element to their particular places, we say: those parts are hypothetical in it, not existing externally, so they do not require a place. And also it is possible that there is a specifying state for the parts with a specific position. And the part of earth is specified to its specific place in which it is only because its matter before that earthly form had another form specifying that part to that place, or specifying it to another place from which that part moved rectilinearly to that place. And the conclusion is that the specifier of that part of earth to its specific place is the previous position obtained for its matter due to a previous form either in that place or in another place from which that part, after the occurrence of its earthly form, moved to its place along the shortest path, and that previous form is preceded by a third form, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: that a specific form or state could determine the place of hyle, and that isolated hyle might be the hyle of a universal element, so no additional specifier is needed. The reply argues that the parts of the element are hypothetical (مفروضة) and do not exist externally, so they do not require a place. It then explains the determination of a specific part of earth through a chain of previous forms and positions, tracing specification back through an infinite regress of forms.

Correction: The reply clarifies that the parts of a universal element are merely hypothetical (مفروضة) and not externally existing, so they do not require a separate specifier.

The specification of actual parts of elements is traced back through an infinite regress of previous forms, reinforcing the need for a first specifier.

The Form's Need for Hyle Through Finitude
Iji's first proof that corporeal form needs hyle, based on finitude of extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof demonstrates that the corporeal form, being a substantial extension, must be finite and therefore requires hyle.

Core Idea
Every corporeal form is extended and finite, so it cannot exist without matter.

In this passage, Iji presents the first of three reasons why the corporeal form itself cannot exist independently of hyle. The argument hinges on the nature of extension: anything extended occupies space and is therefore finite. This finitude implies dependence on a substrate, i.e., hyle. The commentary clarifies the reasoning and links it to earlier discussions about the form's need for indwelling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, including both the main argument and the commentator's remarks. Let the structure sink in.
Notice that the commentator clarifies each phrase of the matn.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms 'مشار إليها' (pointed to) and 'متناه' (finite). Understand their relation to extension.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: form without hyle → must be pointed to → finite → requires hyle. Note the additional point that even if not pointed to, finitude still holds.
This is a proof by disjunction.
4
Understand the importance
Reflect on why this proof is foundational for issues like the eternity of the world. The commentary mentions this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثها أي ثالث التفاريع ( أن الصورة الجسمية أيضاً ( لا تخلو عن الهيولى لوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : لو فرضنا صورة بلا هيولى ) كانت إما مشاراً إليها أو غير مشار إليها فإن كانت مشاراً إليها كان ذلك المشار إليه (متناهياً). قوله : (لكانت إلخ) هذا لا يجوزه العقل بعد ملاحظة أنها امتداد جوهري، فإن الامتداد الجوهري لا يمكن وجوده بدون فراغ يشغله فلا بد أن يكون مشاراً إليه. قوله : (فإن كانت مشاراً إليها كان متناهياً هذه قضية اتفاقية لو لم يكن مشاراً إليها، كانت أيضاً متناهية لأن الثابت بالبراهين تناهي الأبعاد سواء فرض مشار إليها أو لا . بل لا تزيد إلخ) يعني أن المراد الوحدة في المقدار وهي المساواة، لأن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة هذا المطلب وإن علم مما تقدم حيث ثبت أن الصورة بذاتها تقتضي حلول وهو الوجه الثاني بعينه إلا أنه لما كان أصلاً لقدم العالم وغيره من المسائل جعلوه مطلباً برأسه حينئذ إذ في إثباته بالوجه الأول بيان احتياج الصورة إلى المادة والشكل والتساوي ووجوب تناهيها، وأن الهيولى لا تحتاج إلى الصورة المعينة.
Third of them—i.e., the third branch—is that the corporeal form also does not lack hyle, for three reasons. First: If we suppose a form without hyle, it would either be pointed to or not pointed to; if it is pointed to, then that which is pointed to is finite. His saying: ('it would be' etc.)—the intellect does not allow this after noting that it is a substantial extension, for substantial extension cannot exist without a void it occupies, so it must be pointed to. His saying: ('if it is pointed to, it is finite'—this is an agreed-upon proposition; even if it were not pointed to, it would still be finite because it is established by proofs that dimensions are finite whether assumed pointed to or not. Rather, ('does not increase' etc.) means that what is intended is unity in quantity, which is equality, because hyle does not lack form. This point is known from what preceded, where it was established that the form by itself requires indwelling, and that is the very second aspect, except that since it is a foundational matter for the eternity of the world and other issues, they made it an independent topic. For in proving it by the first aspect, there is a demonstration of the form's need for matter, shape, equality, and the necessity of its finitude, and that hyle does not need a specific form.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces the third branch of arguments proving that the corporeal form itself requires hyle. The first proof states that if a form existed without hyle, it could either be pointed to (i.e., occupy space) or not. If it is pointed to, it is finite. The commentator adds that this is axiomatic; even if not pointed to, dimensions are finitely known. He further explains that the term 'does not increase' refers to unity in quantity, which is equality, and that the discussion is a continuation of earlier proofs, particularly the second aspect about the form's need for indwelling. This issue is treated separately due to its role in debates about the eternity of the world.

Misconception: One might think a form could exist without matter as an independent entity, but the argument shows it must be extended and finite, thus needing hyle.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that any corporeal form, being extended, is finite and therefore cannot exist without a material substrate (hyle).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Wax Objection and the Meaning of Connection and Disconnection
Explains the initial objection and its refutation based on the meaning of connection/disconnection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the wax example does not refute the argument that shape change requires connection and disconnection in a continuous body.

Core Idea
Shape change in a continuous body always involves internal separation and connection, even if not visible.

The author defends the first argument against an objection based on wax. The objection claims that wax changes shape without being cut, so connection and disconnection are not necessary. The response clarifies that the intended meaning of connection/disconnection applies to the body's parts, not external cuts. This lesson examines that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objection uses wax as a counterexample. Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully.
Notice the phrase 'as you see in wax'.
2
Understand the two meanings of shape change
The response distinguishes between shape as outer form and shape as formation of bodyness. Connection/disconnection is necessary in the latter sense.
3
Apply the distinction to the wax example
Wax is a continuous body; its shape change involves internal rearrangement of parts, which is a form of separation and connection.
This refutes the objection.

Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the first argument against objections. It begins with a potential objection: shape change does not require connection and disconnection, as seen in wax that takes different shapes without being cut. The author responds by distinguishing the intended meaning: if 'shape' refers to the outer form of a continuous body, then change requires separation or connection; if it refers to the formation of bodyness itself, then shape change implies separation of parts. Then another objection argues that the bodily form could by itself necessitate a specific shape for the whole universe, with an additional concomitant causing wholeness and partiality. The response is that such a concomitant would be necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not its quiddity, so it does not imply separability. Further, the claim that receptivity to shape change implies receptivity to connection/disconnection is rejected as the point of dispute. The passage then discusses imaginary division: accepting shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that this does not undermine the proof but simply offers a shorter route.

الانفصال
الشكل
الشمعة

Correction: Wax is a continuous body; its shape change involves internal rearrangement of parts, which is a form of separation and connection.

C) He denies that wax is a body.
D) He abandons the argument.

Key Takeaway
Shape change in a continuous body always involves internal connection and disconnection, even if not externally visible.

The Bodily Form and the Necessitation of Shape
Addresses the objection about bodily form necessitating shape and the response about a concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodily form could necessitate a specific shape by itself and the response about the concomitant.

Core Idea
The bodily form cannot by itself determine the shape of the universe without an additional concomitant.

A second objection argues that the bodily form itself might necessitate a specific shape for all bodies, but the author responds that such a shape would require an additional concomitant that is necessary for the form's existence, not its quiddity. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection suggests the bodily form could determine shape by itself. Note the claim about the whole universe.
2
Understand the response
The author posits an additional concomitant that causes wholeness and partiality. This concomitant is necessary for the form's existence after abstraction.
The form's quiddity does not require it.
3
Consider the implication
The concomitant does not imply the form can separate from matter; it only explains particularity.

His statement: (An objector may object etc.) This only applies if what is meant by his statement 'it' i.e. shape is only through separation and connection in the body itself; but if what is meant is that the formation of bodyness is only through separation of some parts from others, then it does not apply, as is not hidden, for multiplicity of shapes in extension is only by consideration of separation of some of it, otherwise it would be one extension.
His statement: (So there is nothing except the bodily nature) The restriction is denied. Why is it not allowed that the bodily form by itself necessitates for the totality of the world of bodies a specific shape and a specific magnitude, and that there is another thing concomitant with the bodily form while it is abstracted from hyle, and that thing is the cause of wholeness and partiality. If it is said: Then the form would be receptive to another shape through separation and connection without hyle, which is false. We say: It is denied, for that thing is necessary for the existence of the abstracted form after its abstraction, even if it is not necessary for its quiddity, as has passed.
His statement: (So it is not necessitated from the attribution of shape etc.) It cannot be said: Everything that is receptive to affection and change of shapes is receptive to connection and disconnection, as in wax for example. For we say: We do not concede that change of shapes only occurs through connection and disconnection, for that is the point of dispute. Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the first argument against objections. It begins with a potential objection: shape change does not require connection and disconnection, as seen in wax that takes different shapes without being cut. The author responds by distinguishing the intended meaning: if 'shape' refers to the outer form of a continuous body, then change requires separation or connection; if it refers to the formation of bodyness itself, then shape change implies separation of parts. Then another objection argues that the bodily form could by itself necessitate a specific shape for the whole universe, with an additional concomitant causing wholeness and partiality. The response is that such a concomitant would be necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not its quiddity, so it does not imply separability. Further, the claim that receptivity to shape change implies receptivity to connection/disconnection is rejected as the point of dispute. The passage then discusses imaginary division: accepting shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that this does not undermine the proof but simply offers a shorter route.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الهيولى
الكلية
الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if the bodily form had a concomitant, then it would be receptive to separation from matter.
Correction: The concomitant is necessary for the existence of the abstract form, not for its quiddity, so it does not imply separability.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the concomitant not imply the form's ability to separate from matter?
A) Because the concomitant is part of the form's quiddity.
B) Because the concomitant is necessary only for the form's existence after abstraction.
C) Because the form is material.
D) Because the concomitant is accidental.

Key Takeaway
The bodily form can have a concomitant that causes particularity, but that concomitant is necessary for the form's existence, not its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the bodily form abstracted from matter—a purely intellectual concept. Yet the author insists it still requires a concomitant. What does this reveal about your own life? Identify one area where you believe you can exist independently, without a deeper reality sustaining you. How does this lesson challenge that belief? What will you change today?
?
The passage argues that a thing can have a concomitant necessary for its existence but not its essence. Reflect on a time you tried to separate yourself from something essential to your existence (like a relationship, habit, or belief), thinking it was a separable accident but found it was necessary for your being. How did that lead you to a false conclusion? How does this apply to your dependence on Allah's attributes? What step will you take to realign your understanding?

Imaginary Division and the Independence of Bodyness
Demonstrates that imaginary division leads to disjunctive division and defends the original proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imaginary division leads to disjunctive division and why the premises of the proof are not wasted despite a simpler argument.

Core Idea
Imaginary division in an abstract form would lead to actual separation, which is absurd, so the original proof stands.

The final objection involves imaginary division: if the abstract form is receptive to shape change, it would be subject to imaginary division, which leads to disjunctive division and absurdity. The author concludes that the original proof's premises are not wasted but a shorter path exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about imaginary division
The passage argues that shape change in an abstract form entails imaginary division.
Imaginary division means supposing parts in a simple entity.
2
Understand the consequence
Imaginary division leads to disjunctive division (actual separation), which is absurd for a simple form.
3
See the final point
The author notes that the original proof's premises are not wasted; a shorter argument is available but the original remains valid.

Objection to Form's Independence via Imaginary Division
Objection and answer regarding form's independence via imaginary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that form's independence would entail imaginary division leading to separative division, and the defense that this does not undermine the proof.

Core Idea
Imaginary division is a key test for independence from matter.

In this passage, the objector argues that if form were independent of matter, it would have to accept change of shape, which requires imaginary division. Since imaginary division leads to an impossible conclusion, the claim of independence is refuted. The answer clarifies that this objection does not invalidate the original proof but merely suggests a shorter path.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the objector's argument: form's independence would mean it can accept division and connection, leading to shape changes that entail imaginary division.
The objector uses the term 'القسمة الوهمية' (imaginary division).
2
Trace the Consequence
Note that imaginary division leads to separative division, which is considered impossible. This is the core of the objection.
The objector refers to a previously established rule that imaginary division leads to separative division.
3
Examine the Answer
The answer states that this objection does not refute the proof; it only points out a shorter argument. The original proof's premises are still sound.
The answer emphasizes that the objection is about economy of argument, not logical validity.
4
Understand the Gloss on Shape
The gloss clarifies that shape (shakl) requires extension, so imaginary division only applies to things with a specific nature having multiple individuals.
This limits the scope of the objection.

Thus all these premises mentioned in your proof are wasted, unnecessary. Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer within the argument for the existence of hyle. The objector claims that if form were independent of matter, it would be capable of accepting change of shape, which requires imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya). The objector argues that imaginary division leads to separative division (al-infikakiyya), which is impossible. The author of the objection asserts that their premises are sufficient and not wasted. The answer provided is that this does not undermine the soundness of the discourse; rather it is a matter of choosing a shorter path. The gloss explains that shape (shakl) requires extension, and imaginary division only applies to things with a specific nature having multiple individuals.

Key Terms

القسمة الوهمية (imaginary division)
القسمة الانفكاكية (separative division)
شكل (shape)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That imaginary division cannot apply to forms because they are immaterial.
Correction: The passage shows that if form were independent, it would have to accept change of shape, which entails imaginary division. But the answer clarifies that shape requires extension, so imaginary division only applies to extended things. Thus the objection is not relevant to truly immaterial forms.

Checkpoint

A) Imaginary division
B) Separative division
C) Form's independence
D) The body's division

Key Takeaway
The objection that form's independence would lead to impossible imaginary and separative divisions is answered by noting that it does not undermine the original proof but only points to a shorter argument.

?

The Second Argument: Corporeal Form's Need for Hyle
Second argument: form's inherence proves its need for matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument that corporeal form cannot exist independently and must inhere in hyle.

Core Idea
If form were independent, it would not need a substrate—but it does inhere, so it cannot be independent.

This lesson presents the second of three arguments demonstrating the necessity of hyle. The argument uses a conditional: if corporeal form were self-subsisting, it would have no need for a substrate; yet we observe it inheres in bodies, contradicting independence. This reasoning establishes the essential dependence of form on matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the conditional logic of the argument. Notice the structure: 'if... then... but... therefore...'.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'corporeal form', 'hyle', and 'substrate' in the passage. Understand how each functions in the argument.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the assumption of independence leads to a contradiction. This shows that form must depend on a substrate.
This is a classic kalam proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( من الوجوه الثلاثة ( الصورة الجسمية (لو) خلت عن الهيولى و (قامت بذاتها لاستغنت في نفسها ( عن المحل فلا تحل فيه) أصلاً لكنها حالة فيه فلا يجوز حلولها عنه وقد عرفت جوابه . قوله : ( وقد عرفت جوابه من أنه يجوز أن لا تكون محتاجة ولا مستعيرة . قوله : ( وقد عرفت جوابه وقد عرفت أيضاً ما في هذا الجواب من أنه لا واسطة بين الاحتياج الذاتي إلى المحل والغنى الذاتي عنه، وقد عرفت أيضاً ما ذكرنا في توجيه كلام المصنف .
The second (of the three aspects): The corporeal form, if it were devoid of hyle and subsisted by itself, it would be independent in itself of a substrate, so it would not inhere in it at all; but it is inherent in it, so its inherence in it cannot be revoked; and you have known its answer. His statement: (and you have known its answer) that it is permissible that it is neither needy nor borrowing. His statement: (and you have known its answer and you have also known what there is in this answer: that there is no intermediate between essential need for a substrate and essential independence from it; and you have also known what we mentioned in directing the author's words).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the necessity of hyle. The argument states that if the corporeal form were independent of hyle and subsisted by itself, it would not need a substrate and therefore would not inhere in anything. But in fact it does inhere in bodies, so it cannot be independent. The answer, referenced as already known, is that it is possible for the form to be neither in need of a substrate nor borrowing one. The gloss elaborates that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence, and this is part of the author's explanation.

The second argument uses a conditional to prove that corporeal form cannot subsist without hyle, because independence would negate its observed inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that for any corporeal form to exist, it must depend on a material substrate. This means nothing in the physical world is self-sufficient. As you look at your own body, its shape, color, and health, do you truly believe they are gifts from Allah that depend entirely on Him? Or do you sometimes act as if they are independent? ?

Does this argument strengthen your conviction that the universe is created and not eternal? Consider one thing you use daily (your phone, your car): its form and matter are combined by someone. Are you grateful to the Creator for combining the atoms of your body into a human form? What is one practical way you can show gratitude today?

Answering the Argument and Its Critique
Answer and critique regarding the necessity of a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the second argument and the critique that there is no middle state between essential need and independence.

Core Idea
The answer claims form might be neither needy nor borrowing, but the critique says there is no middle ground.

After presenting the second argument, the text references an answer that has been previously given: the form could be neither in need of a substrate nor borrowing one. However, the author then points out a flaw in that answer, namely that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence. This lesson explores the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'يجوز أن لا تكون محتاجة ولا مستعيرة' (it is permissible that it is neither needy nor borrowing).
This is the proposed answer.
2
Understand the critique
The critique states 'لا واسطة بين الاحتياج الذاتي إلى المحل والغنى الذاتي عنه' (there is no intermediate between essential need and essential independence).
This is a key logical principle.
3
Identify key terms
Note the terms 'احتياج ذاتي' (essential need) and 'غنى ذاتي' (essential independence). They are opposites with no gradation.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider why the answer fails: if there is no middle state, then the form must be either in need or independent. The answer's third option is invalid.
This shows the rigor of kalam reasoning.

Third Argument: No Distinction Without Matter
Third argument against form without matter based on loss of distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that form cannot be differentiated into whole and part without matter.

Core Idea
Without matter, whole and part become indistinguishable.

This lesson presents the third argument against form existing independently of matter. It examines the logical implications of separating form from matter, specifically regarding the distinction between the whole and its parts. The argument shows that such distinction is only possible through matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation. Note the structure: assumption, hypothetical scenario, two logical possibilities, and the gloss.
The passage includes both the main argument and a clarifying commentary.
2
Understand the scenario
The argument supposes that form could exist without matter. It then imagines a composite whole (hyle+form) and considers what happens if its form separates before and after division. Focus on the meaning of 'whole' and 'form of the whole'.
The 'whole' here is the composite, not the form alone.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
If there is no distinction between the form of the whole and the form of the part, an absurdity follows: the thing with another is like it without. If there is a distinction, then earlier principles on individuation tell us that no distinction or multiplicity exists without matter. Both options lead to problems for the idea of form without matter.
4
Examine the commentator's clarification
The gloss explains that wholeness and partness are due to matter. If the form is isolated, there is no whole or part, so the absurdity does not actually arise because the scenario is incoherent. The argument ultimately shows that the initial assumption (form without matter) is impossible because it leads to a paradox.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( من تلك الوجوه أن يقول : على تقدير أن يجوز خلو الصورة عن المادة ( نفرض الكل تفارقه صورته قبل التجزئة وبعدها فإن كان لا تميز ثمة بين صورة الكل وصورة الجزء ( فالشيء مع غيره كهو لا وإن كان ) بينهما تميز ( وقد عرفت ) في مباحث التعين ( أنه لا تميز) ولا تعدد قوله : (فإن كان لا تميز (إلخ فيه أن الكلية والجزئية باعتبار المادة فإذا فرضت الصورة منفردة عن المادة فلا كل ولا جزء ولا تعدد فيها ولا يلزم أن يكون الشيء مع غيره كهو لا معه فتدبر . قوله : ( نفرض الكل تفارقه صورته المراد من الكل هاهنا المجموع المركب من الهيولى والصورة وقوله : صورته هذه الإضافة من قبيل إضافة الجزء إلى الكل ومعنى مفارقة الصورة عن الكل هو أن تبقى الصورة بدون الكل فتكون حينئذ مجردة عن الهيولى وقوله : قبل التجزئة وبعدها متعلق بقوله: تفارقه، ثم إن إمكان وقوع المجموع الذي فرضتم ممنوع في هوية الصورة التي فرض كونها مجردة عن الهيولى لا بد لإثباته من دليل .
The third (of those aspects) is to say: on the assumption that it is permissible for the form to be devoid of matter, we suppose the whole whose form separates from it before and after division. Then if there is no distinction there between the form of the whole and the form of the part, then the thing with another is like it without; and if there is a distinction between them, and you have known in the investigations of individuation that there is no distinction and no multiplicity. His statement: (if there is no distinction etc.) in it is that wholeness and partness are by virtue of matter; so if the form is supposed isolated from matter, then there is no whole, no part, and no multiplicity in it; and it does not follow that the thing with another is like it without it, so reflect. His statement: (we suppose the whole whose form separates from it) what is meant by 'the whole' here is the composite of hyle and form; and his statement 'its form' is an addition of a part to the whole; and the meaning of the separation of the form from the whole is that the form remains without the whole, thus it is then abstracted from hyle; and his statement 'before and after division' is attached to his statement 'separates from it'; then the possibility of occurrence of the whole that you supposed is untenable regarding the identity of the form that is supposed to be abstracted from hyle; a proof is needed for its establishment.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. It posits that if form could exist without matter, we could imagine a whole whose form separates from it before and after division. If there is no distinction between the form of the whole and the form of the part, then the thing with another is identical to itself without the other, which is absurd. If there is a distinction, then earlier investigations on individuation show that there is no distinction or multiplicity without matter. The gloss explains that wholeness and partness depend on matter; so if form is isolated, there is no whole or part, and the absurdity does not follow. The gloss also clarifies that the 'whole' refers to the composite of hyle and form, and the separation means the form remains without the whole, requiring proof.

جزء (part)

تعدد (multiplicity)
تعين (individuation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument proves that form cannot be separated at all, but it actually shows that without matter, the distinction between whole and part collapses.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming form without matter leads to an incoherent scenario where whole and part are indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument claim would happen if form were abstracted from matter?
A) Form would have multiple parts
B) There would be no distinction between whole and part
C) Form would become eternal
D) The body would cease to exist

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that without matter, the concepts of whole and part lose their meaning, thereby refuting the possibility of form existing independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your body were to decay, what remains of ‘you’? How does this affect your preparation for the afterlife?
?
Consider that the assumption of form without matter leads to contradiction. Are there areas in your life where you try to separate your inner beliefs (form) from your outward actions (matter)? How might this disconnect lead to spiritual inconsistency or hypocrisy?

Causal Independence of Hyle and Form
Sanusi argues that hyle is not an efficient cause of form, nor form a cause of hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that hyle and form are not causes of each other in the composition of body.

Core Idea
Hyle and form are not causes of each other; they are mutually dependent without circular causality.

In this passage, Sanusi examines whether hyle (matter) or form serves as the cause of the other in the composition of body. Drawing on principles from the investigation of quiddity, he argues that true composition requires a mutual need without circularity. He then presents three reasons why hyle cannot be an efficient cause of form, and one reason why form cannot be a cause of hyle, clarifying the nature of their mutual dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of arguments.
Notice the structure: first a general principle about composite quiddities, then specific arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments against hyle as cause
The passage gives three reasons: (1) hyle would precede form, (2) hyle would be both agent and recipient, (3) hyle would cause a specific form arbitrarily. Find each.
The glosses clarify that hyle is not truly simple.
3
Understand the argument against form as cause
Form inheres in hyle so it needs hyle; thus form cannot be a cause of hyle. Consider the implication and the gloss about need for agent vs. recipient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها أي رابع تفريعات الهيولى وتركب الجسم منها ومن الصورة ( قد علمت ) في مباحث الماهية ( أنه لا بد ) في الماهية الحقيقية المركبة ( من احتياج أحد الجزأين إلى الآخر) فقط أو احتياج كل منهما إلى صاحبه على وجه لا يلزم منه دور، وحينئذ فلا بد بين جزأي الجسم من حاجة وأما كيفية تلك الحاجة ( فاعلم أن الهيولى ليست علة للصورة، وإلا لتم لها أي للهيولى ( وجود قبل وجود الصورة ) لأن العلة متقدمة بالوجود على معلولها لكنا قد بينا أن المادة لا تكون بالفعل إلا بسبب الصورة لأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون متصفاً بالقوة والفعل معاً، وقد عرفت فساده فلا نعيده (و) أيضا لو كانت الهيولى علة للصورة ( لاجتمع فيها ) أي في الهيولى (القبول والفعل بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد فإنها حينئذ فاعلة للصورة وقابلة لها وهو باطل، وجوابه أنه مبني على أن البسيط لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً علمت ما فيه (و) أيضاً لا يجوز أن تكون الهيولى علة للصورة ( لأنها ) في حد ذاتها تقبل صوراً لا نهاية لها فلا تكون علة (للمعينة أي لا تكون علة لمعينة من الصور حتى يكون حصولها في الهيولى أولى من حصول غيرها دفعاً للتحكم، بل ليس للمادة إلا مجرد القبول، وأما سبب حصول الصورة المعينة فيها فأمر آخر ( ولا الصورة) أي وليس الصورة أيضاً علة ( للهيولى لأنها حالة فيها فتحتاج) الصورة ( في وجودها إليها ويتجه على هذه العبارة أنه يلزم حينئذ كون الهيولى علة للصورة قوله : ( في الماهية الحقيقية أي المتصفة بالوحدة الحقيقية أي الوحدة في الخارج. قوله : (ليست علة ) أي علة فاعلية . قوله : ( مبني على أن البسيط إلخ ) مع أن الهيولي ليست بسيطاً حقيقياً. قوله : ( ويتجه على هذه العبارة إلخ فيه أن المثبت هاهنا الاحتياج إلى الفاعل والمنفي فيما سبق الاحتياج إلى القابل. قوله: (فلا تكون علة للمعينة لم لا يجوز أن يكون علة للمعينة لا لذاتها. بل بشرط خارج عن ذاتها منضم إليها فلا يلزم التحكم وأما عدم كونها قابلاً وفاعلاً فقد عرفت ما فيه قوله : ( ويتجه على هذه العبارة أنه يلزم حينئذ كون الهيولى علة للصورة) إذ المحتاج إليه لا بد أن يكون علة للمجتاج، فإن معنى العلة هاهنا هو ما يحتاج إلى الشيء في وجوده في نفسه لكن الصورة لم تكن محتاجة في وجودها في نفسها إلى المحل الذي هو الهيولى وإلا يلزم أن
The fourth of them, i.e. the fourth of the subdivisions of hyle and the composition of the body from it and from form: (you have known) in the investigations of quiddity that in a true composite quiddity there must be a need of one of the two parts for the other only, or a need of each for its companion in a way that does not entail a circle. Then there must be a need between the two parts of the body. As for the manner of that need, (know that hyle is not a cause of form, otherwise it would have existence before the existence of form, because the cause is prior in existence to its effect; but we have shown that matter is actual only by virtue of form, because one thing cannot be qualified by potency and act simultaneously, and you have known its falsity so we do not repeat it. And also if hyle were a cause of form, then acceptance and act would be combined in it, i.e. in hyle, with respect to one thing, for then it would be agent of form and recipient of it, which is false. Its answer is that it is based on the idea that the simple cannot be both recipient and agent together; you have known what is in it. And also it is not permissible for hyle to be a cause of form because it in itself receives infinite forms, so it cannot be a cause of a specific [form], i.e. it cannot be a cause of a specific form such that its occurrence in hyle is more fitting than another, to avoid arbitrariness; rather matter has only mere receptivity; as for the cause of the occurrence of the specific form in it, it is another matter. And neither is form a cause of hyle, because it inheres in it, so form needs it in its existence. And this expression implies that then hyle would be a cause of form. His statement: (in the true quiddity) i.e. qualified by true unity, i.e. unity in the external. His statement: (is not a cause) i.e. efficient cause. His statement: (built on the idea that the simple etc.) even though hyle is not truly simple. His statement: (and this expression implies etc.) in it is that what is being affirmed here is need for the agent, while what was negated earlier was need for the recipient. His statement: (so it cannot be a cause of a specific form) why may it not be a cause of a specific form not by itself, but with a condition external to it attached to it, so arbitrariness does not follow? As for it not being both recipient and agent, you have known what is in it. His statement: (and this expression implies that then hyle would be a cause of form) because that which is needed must be a cause of the needy, for the meaning of cause here is that which the thing needs in its existence in itself; but form is not in need in its own existence of the substrate which is hyle, otherwise it would be necessary that...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth subdivision regarding the relationship between hyle and form. It first establishes that in a true composite, each part needs the other in a non-circular way. Then it argues that hyle is not an efficient cause of form because: (1) if hyle were a cause, it would have existence prior to form, but matter is actual only through form, and a thing cannot be both potential and actual. (2) If hyle were a cause, it would be both agent (giving form) and recipient (receiving form), which is impossible for a simple being. (3) Hyle cannot be a cause of a specific form because it receives infinite forms; specifying one would be arbitrary unless an external factor determines it. Similarly, form is not a cause of hyle because form inheres in hyle and needs it. The glosses clarify that hyle is not truly simple, and the need here is for an agent, not a recipient; also that form is not in need of a substrate in its own essence.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
هيولى (hyle)
صورة (form)
قبول (receptivity/acception)
فعل (act/agency)
بسيط (simple)
تحكم (arbitrariness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because hyle receives form, it causes form to exist.
Correction: Sanusi argues that reception is not agency; matter is merely receptive, and the cause of form is external.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason given that hyle cannot be a cause of form?
A) Hyle would then exist before form
B) Hyle would then be both agent and recipient
C) Hyle receives infinite forms
D) Form is eternal and uncreated

Key Takeaway
Neither hyle nor form causes the other; their relation is one of mutual need without circular causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that hyle cannot cause form because it is merely receptive. In your own life, are there areas where you pretend to be the cause of your own characteristics, when in reality you are only receiving them from an external source—your Creator? How would your daily habits change if you truly felt that your specific form is determined by Allah, not by yourself?
?
The lesson shows that form does not cause its substrate; it inheres in it. Are there attachments or behaviors that you think define you (your 'forms') that you actually 'cause' and control? What if the real cause is your need for the substrate—your human weakness and dependence? Will you let go of the illusion of self-causation and turn to the One who truly gives form to your life?
